About this Manual
This manual describes the SCANLAB 
RTC4 boards and their usage for synchronous control 
of scan systems, lasers and peripheral equipment. 
The main chapters of this manual use the 
RTC4 PCIe Board to exemplify. For a product overview 
see Chapter 2, “RTC4 PCIe Board – Product 
Overview”, Page 17.
The Appendix describes an additional RTC4 hardware 
variant: 
• RTC4 Ethernet Board, see page 214 
This manual is a part of the product. Read this manual 
carefully before you proceed with installation and 
operation of RTC4 boards. Contact SCANLAB, if there 
are any questions regarding the contents of this 
manual, see Chapter 1.1 “Manufacturer”. 
Keep this manual available for maintenance, care, 
repair and disposal. This manual should accompany 
the product if ownership of the RTC4 board changes 
hands. 
This manual refers to the following versions(1): 
• RTC4 DLL 
RTC4DLL.dll und RTC4DLLx64.dll, V400 
• DSP Program Files 
RTC4D2.HEX V2.400 
RTC4D3.HEX V3.400 
• RTC4 firmware 
V128 
You can query your version numbers by: 
• get_dll_version 
• get_hex_version 
• get_rtc_version

Glossary and Abbreviations
AOM 
Acousto-optic modulator. 
arc[*] Command
See Chapter 4.2.2, “arc[*] Commands”, Page 29. 
RTC4 boards only support arc[*] Commands (but no ellipse 
commands). 
DSP 
Digital signal processor on the RTC4 board. 
DSP Program File 
• RTC4D2.HEX = DSP Program File for 2D user programs 
• RTC4D3.HEX = DSP Program File for 3D user programs 
Ethernet Link Loss 
The cable connection to the RTC4 Ethernet Board has been 
disconnected. 
Hardware Reset 
New start after powering the RTC6 board. 
Synonym: “power up”, “power cycle“. 
High-Bandwidth Return Channel 
Multiplexing 
Functionality not yet released.
iDRIVE Scan Systems 
In this manual, the term subsumes, for example, the following 
SCANLAB products: intelliSCAN, intelliSCANde, intelliSCANse, 
intelliDRILL, intellicube, intelliWELD, varioSCANde, 
powerSCAN II 50i, excelliSCAN. 
Image Field 
Synonym: Working Field. 
intelliSCAN 
In this manual, the term subsumes, for example, the following 
SCANLAB products: intelliSCAN, intelliSCANde, intelliSCANse, 
intelliDRILL, intellicube, intelliWELD, powerSCAN II 50i. 
jump[*] Command 
See also Section ”jump[*] Commands”, Page 28. 
Serves to move the scan system axes to a new position while the 
laser is off. 
LSB 
Least Significant Bit. 
mark[*] Command
See Section ”mark[*] Commands”, Page 28. 
Marking Command 
Umbrella term for RTC4 commands that perform marking motions 
while the laser is switched on. 
Examples: mark[*] Command, arc[*] Command. 
Microstep 
See Chapter 4.2.3, “Microstepping”, Page 29. 
Microvector 
The term refers to micro_vector[*] commands of RTC5 and RTC6. 
MSB 
Most Significant Bit.

innovators for industry
NULL 
Means on the one hand the number 0, on the other hand a pointer 
with the value 0. The spelling for this is different in the different 
programming languages.
null-terminated 
“Terminated with a null character as end identifier” (a character with 
the internal value zero, often called “NUL”, not the same as the glyph 
zero). 
PCB 
Printed Circuit Board. 
Pixel Mode 
Brief for “Pixel Output Mode”. See Chapter 5.7, 
“Pixel Output Mode”, Page 67. 
Polyline 
A direct sequence of mark[*] Commands. The marking is 
continuous. Synonym: polygonal chain, polygonal line, polygonal 
traversal. 
PosAck 
“Position Acknowledge”, see PosAck Signal. 
PosAck Signal 
• As of scan system firmware 2001. 
• PosAck Signal-capable SCANLAB scan systems, 
for example, intelliSCAN 
• Refers to the meaning of: 
– Bit #12, Bit #4, Bit #11, Bit #3 of the XY2-100 status word 
transferred with 16-bit protocol 
– Bit #16, Bit #8, Bit #15, Bit #7 of the XY2-100 status word 
transferred with 20-bit protocol 
• These bits are set, if the position errors of x axis and y axis are in 
the allowed range: 
– See also “excelliSCAN Scan Heads – Functional Principle of 
SCANahead Servo Control and Operation by RTC6 Boards” 
Manual, Chapter 2.1.3, “TrAck Signal”, Page 19. 
– For more information, refer to the corresponding scan head 
manuals. 
• Compare to TrAck Signal. 
Preprocessing Servo Control 
Servo control (of newer generation) in Preprocessing Systems 
= with Tracking Error and with preprocessing. 
Preprocessing System 
SCANLAB scan system with Preprocessing Servo Control. 
Compare to Tracking Error System, SCANahead System. 
Reset of the RTC4 Board 
Synonym: Software Reset. 
SCANahead Servo Control 
Equipment feature of certain SCANLAB scan systems 
(= “SCANahead Systems”), essentially based on an ISB1 as a servo 
board for the 2 galvanometer scanner with corresponding firmware 
and parameterization. 
Refer to “excelliSCAN Scan Heads – Functional Principle of 
SCANahead Servo Control and Operation by RTC6 Boards” Manual. 
SCANahead System 
SCANLAB scan system with SCANahead Servo Control, for example, 
excelliSCAN series. 
Compare to Tracking Error System, Preprocessing System.

innovators for industry
Software Reset 
Restart after load_program_file. This does not reset everything, for 
example, loaded correction tables are retained. See also dsp_start. 
Synonym: Reset of the RTC4 Board. 
TrAck 
“Trajectory Acknowledge”, see TrAck Signal. 
TrAck Signal 
• As of scan system firmware 5102 + 5112. 
• TrAck Signal-capable SCANLAB scan systems, 
for example, SCANahead Systems 
• Refers to the meaning of: 
– Bit #12, Bit #4, Bit #11, Bit #3 of the XY2-100 status word 
transferred with 16-bit protocol 
– Bit #16, Bit #8, Bit #15, Bit #7 of the XY2-100 status word 
transferred with 20-bit protocol 
• These bits are set, if the Trajectory Errors of x axis and y axis are 
in the allowed range: 
– See also “excelliSCAN Scan Heads – Functional Principle of 
SCANahead Servo Control and Operation by RTC6 Boards” 
Manual, Chapter 2.1.3, “TrAck Signal”, Page 19. 
– For more information, refer to the corresponding scan head 
manuals. 
• Compare to PosAck Signal. 
Tracking Error 
Time difference between the planned and actual reaching of a 
certain mirror position at constant speed. 
Tracking Error Servo Control 
The “conventional” servo control of Tracking Error Systems. 
Tracking Error System 
SCANLAB scan system with conventional control (1st generation) = 
with Tracking Error and without preprocessing. 
For example, intelliSCAN III. 
Compare to SCANahead System, Preprocessing System. 
Trajectory 
In this manual: curve with 10 µs parameterization. 
Trajectory Error 
Deviation of the actual Trajectory from the set Trajectory. 
Vector Command 
Umbrella term for jump[*] Commands and mark[*] Commands. 
See also Chapter 4.2.1, “Vector Commands”, Page 28. 
Working Field 
Synonym: Image Field. 
XY2-100 Converter (Accessory) 
Refer to the RTC6-Manual, Chapter , 
“XY2-100 Converter (Accessory)”, Page 16.

RTC4 PCIe Board – Product Overview
2.1
Labeling
The article number and individual configuration of 
your RTC4 board can be found in a separate delivered 
file. See also “Type Identification”, Page 17 and 
Chapter 7, “Options”, Page 93. 
The serial number of the RTC4 PCIe Board is printed 
on a label attached to the board (format: “SN…”). 
2.2
Type Identification
The part number and a six-digit type designation 
correspond to a specific factory configuration of 
jumpers X3…X11. 
2.3
Unpacking Instructions and 
Typical Package Contents
(1) Carefully remove the RTC4 PCIe Board from the 
package.
(2) Keep the packaging, including the antistatic bag 
the RTC4 PCIe Board is delivered in, so that in case 
of repair the board can be properly repackaged 
and returned to SCANLAB.
(3) Also remove all other articles from the package. 
Check that all parts have been delivered. Refer to 
the corresponding packaging list.
The package typically includes an 
RTC4 PCIe Board and a data storage medium 
(containing the corresponding software (see 
below) and this manual). 
2.4
Supplied 
RTC4 Software Package
Important: Observe the scope of delivery and 
installation description on your data storage medium 
– in English: Readme.txt 
– in German: Liesmich.txt 
Typically, in several subfolders there are provided: 
• Correction files (with cor_1to1.ctb; optionally: 
additional correction files for 2-/3-axis systems) 
• Drivers for the latest Microsoft 32 bit and 64 bit 
operating systems 
• Drivers for legacy Microsoft operating systems 
• For the software development: the Dynamic Link 
Libraries (32 bit RTC4DLL.dll and 64 bit 
RTC4DLLx64.dll) specific for your board, and 
among other things import declarations in 
several programming languages
• Demo files (with example source code(s) for 
user programs) 
• HPGL Converter 
HPGL.exe 
• Other files: 
– iSCANcfg diagnosis tool for iDRIVE technology 
scan heads 
– getRTC diagnosis tool for RTC installation check 
– DSP Program File for 2D user programs 
RTC4D2.HEX 
– DSP Program File for 3D user programs 
RTC4D3.HEX 
Tip: You can download the software package at any 
time from the SCANLAB website.

2 RTC4 PCIe Board – Product Overview 
18
innovators for industry
2.5
Intended Use
The SCANLAB RTC4 PCIe Board is designed for real-
time control of scan heads and lasers by a Windows 
PC with a PCI bus interface.
The RTC4 PCIe Board is based on a DSP providing full 
real-time control for a wide range of applications, 
including enhanced double scan head control and 
runtime image transformations.
The included software provides an extensive set of 
control commands. The RTC4 PCIe Board stores and 
processes these commands completely 
independently of the host PC. This makes it possible 
to meet the stringent demands of real-time 
scan head and laser control even if the PC must 
simultaneously respond to other tasks such as 
machine control and network communication.
The RTC4 PCIe Board offers various 
laser control signals for all commonly used laser 
types. Users can select from five different 
laser modes. In addition, the output signals can be 
customized to meet special requirements.
The standard version of the RTC4 PCIe Board includes
• a 16-bit digital input port 
• a 16-bit digital output port 
• an 8-bit digital output port 
• two versatile analog output ports with 10-bit 
resolution 
The following options are available: 
• Control signals for simultaneous control of two 
scan heads, with individual Image 
Field correction for each scan head = Option 
“Second Scan Head Control”
• Control signals for Processing-on-the-fly 
applications = Option Processing-on-the-fly 
• Control signals for a third axis, for example, a 
varioSCAN = Option “3D” 
• Optical Data Interface 
• I/O Extension Board for RTC3/RTC4 (Accessory)
Only hardware extensions from SCANLAB should be 
used in combination with RTC4 boards.
In addition to the RTC4 PCIe Board the following 
hardware variant is deliverable: 
• RTC4 Ethernet Board, see page 214.
Caution!
• Do not operate the RTC4 board outside of the 
PC.
• The RTC4 board is intended only for industrial 
usage. It is designed to be incorporated in 
machines (normally laser systems). It does not 
meet all criteria of ready-to-use products. Do 
not operate the RTC4 board unless it is 
incorporated in a machine which itself complies 
with the regulations of all applicable standards 
and directives (of the country concerned). It is 
not suitable to be used as toy, in household or 
under unfavourable environment conditions 
(for example, in the open). Appropriate 
precautions to avoid such unforeseeable 
misapplications must be taken by users.
• Installation and operation must only be 
performed by trained specialists, among other 
things knowledgeable in the safe and proper 
use of electrical devices. Only carry out 
installation and maintenance work when supply 
voltages and lasers have been switched off.
• The RTC4 board is a class A product. In a 
domestic environment this product may cause 
radio interferences in which case users may be 
required to take adequate measures.

[p.19]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
2 RTC4 PCIe Board – Product Overview 
19
innovators for industry
2.6
System Requirements
2.6.1
Hardware
The RTC4 PCIe Board requires a Windows PC with a 
PCIe bus interface and at least one free PCIe slot. The 
dimensions of the RTC4 PCIe Board are shown in 
Figure 1.
A second PCIe slot is required, if the I/O Extension 
Board for RTC3/RTC4 (Accessory) is to be used (if even 
possible, see Notice!, Page 94). 
2.6.2
Software
Drivers for the 32-bit and 64-bit Microsoft operating 
systems Windows 10, 8, 7 are included in the 
package. 
They support the RTC4's plug and play facility and the 
operation of up to 16 RTC4 boards in a single PC.
Note: 
• Users of 64-Bit Microsoft Windows operating 
systems must use 
Driver version 6.1.7600.16385 
Driver date March 18, 2016 (or newer)(1). 
Notice!
• RTC3 and RTC4 boards cannot be operated in 
the same PC. 
(1) To identify the driver version in the Device Manager 
(Windows 7): press Windows key +r, enter mmc 
devmgmt.msc and click OK. Then double-click the RTC4 in 
the structure tree. Driver version and driver date can be 
found on the Driver tab.
Caution!
• For Windows PCs with 
Driver version 6.1.7600.16385 
Driver date March 18, 2016 (or newer) 
installed(1), the following must be observed: 
Once the RTC4 PCIe Board is in use by an 
RTC4 application, its power supply must not be 
turned off under any circumstances. 
Otherwise, all non-permanent settings of the 
RTC4 PCIe Board are lost. Therefore, take 
appropriate actions that the Sleep and 
Hibernate commands in the Windows Start 
menu are disabled (see Figure 33 and 
Chapter 10.1) while the RTC4 PCIe Board is in 
use by an RTC4 application.
• For Windows PCs with 
Driver version 6.1.7600.16385 
Driver date March 18, 2016 (or newer) 
not installed(1)(a), the following must be 
observed.
The RTC4 PCIe Board does not support power-
saving modes, which switch off the power 
supplies of the PCIe bus. According standby or 
sleep modes of the operating system must be 
deactivated.
(a) For example, driver V.2.2.0.

2.8
Notes for RTC3 Users
In this Chapter: 
• Chapter 2.8.1, “Hardware Changes”, Page 22 
• Chapter 2.8.2, “Notes on the Installation”, 
Page 22 
• Chapter 2.8.3, “Changes in the Command Set”, 
Page 22 
2.8.1
Hardware Changes
• Standard RTC4 PCIe Boards boards are equipped 
with an EXTENSION 1 socket connector. This 
socket connector provides a 16-bit digital input 
and a 16-bit digital output (previously only 
available by an optional RTC3 I/O Board) as well 
as access to the BUSY status signal, see 
Chapter 6.7, “EXTENSION 1 Socket Connector”, 
Page 90.
• RTC4 PCIe Boards are optionally available with an 
optical data interface, see Chapter 6.6, “Optical 
Data Interface (Optional)”, Page 89. This 
eliminates the need for an add-on board to 
implement optical data transmission between the 
PC interface board and the scan system’s 
(optional) integrated optical data interface.
2.8.2
Notes on the Installation
• RTC3 and RTC4 boards cannot be operated in the 
same PC!
• Follow the instructions given in the supplied 
Readme.txt file.
• Also follow the guidance in Chapter 9, 
“Installation and Start-Up”, Page 95.
2.8.3
Changes in the Command Set
New Commands
• The new arc[*] Commands support marking with 
basic arcs, see Chapter 4.2.2, 
“arc[*] Commands”, Page 29.
• Commands for Status Monitoring and 
Diagnostics are suitable for both monitoring the 
operational status and diagnosing the 
scan system, see Chapter 4.7, “Status Monitoring 
and Diagnostics”, Page 54.
• The 16-bit digital input and 16-bit digital output 
at the EXTENSION 1 socket connector can be 
queried or set by I/O commands, see 
Chapter 6.7.1, “16-Bit Digital Input and 
16-Bit Digital Output”, Page 90. 
Some of these I/O commands can execute 
conditional jumps dependent on the current 
value of the digital input, thereby expanding 
support for structured programming, see 
Chapter 5.6.3, “Conditional List Jumps”, Page 65. 
These commands were previously only available 
with the optional RTC3 I/O Board. 
Changed Commands
In the RTC4 command set, rtc4_count_cards 
replaces the RTC3 command rtc3_count_cards.

3 Safety During Installation and Operation 
23
innovators for industry
3
Safety During Installation and Operation
Read these operating instructions completely before 
you proceed with installing and operating the RTC4.
If there are any questions regarding the contents of 
this manual, contact SCANLAB.
The following conventions apply to safety notices in 
this manual: 
3.1
Safe Operation
3.2
Laser Safety
The RTC4 is intended for controlling a laser 
scan system. Therefore, all relevant laser safety 
directives must be known and applied before 
installation and operation. The customer is solely 
responsible for ensuring the laser safety of the entire 
system.
• Safety notices which draw attention 
to severely injuries or even death are 
identified by the hazard symbol and 
the signal word “Warning!“. 
• Safety notices which draw attention 
to a health hazard are identified by 
the hazard symbol and the signal 
word “Caution!“. 
• Safety notices that recommend 
proper use of the device or warn 
against possible damage to property 
are (without hazard sign) only 
identified by the signal word 
“Notice!“. 
Notice!
• Carefully check your user program before 
running it. Programming errors can cause a 
break down of the system. In this case neither 
the laser nor the scan system can be controlled. 
• Protect the board from humidity, dust, corrosive 
vapors and mechanical stress. 
• For storage and operation of the board, avoid 
electromagnetic fields and static electricity. 
These can damage the electronics on the board. 
For storage, always use the antistatic bag the 
board is delivered in. 
• The allowed operating temperature range is 
15 °C to 60 °C. 
• The storage temperature should be 
between –20 °C and +60 °C. 
Caution!
• All applicable laser safety directives must be 
adhered to. Safety regulations may differ from 
country to country. It is the responsibility of the 
customer to comply with all local regulations.
• Observe all laser safety instructions as described 
in your scan system manual, chapter “Safety 
during Installation and Operation“.
• Always turn on the PC and the power supply for 
the scan head first before turning on the laser. 
Otherwise there is the danger of uncontrolled 
deflection of the laser beam.
SCANLAB recommends the use of a shutter 
to prevent uncontrolled emission of 
laser radiation. 
Notice!

[p.24]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
4 RTC4 Principle of Operation 
24
innovators for industry
4
RTC4 Principle of Operation
4.1
Software Concept
Figure 3 shows a simple laser marking sample(1).
The image is made up of straight line segments or 
vectors. The RTC4 software provides a set of 
jump[*] Commands and mark[*] Commands for 
drawing such vector images. Each of these 
commands describes one vector. The RTC4 software 
provides arc[*] Commands for producing circular 
arcs. Additional software commands are available for 
controlling the laser during the marking process.
The RTC4 processes the commands it receives and 
precisely transmits the required marking signals to 
the scan head using a predefined 10 µs time raster 
and to the laser. The scan head’s 
galvanometer scanners accurately position their 
deflection mirrors in synchronization with the 
incoming control signals. The control signals are 
transferred to the galvanometer scanners digitally in 
accordance with the industry standard XY2-100 (or 
for optical data transfer in accordance with the 
XY2-100-O protocol).
Current scan head status information can be queried 
by the RTC4, also in accordance with the XY2-100 (or 
XY2-100-O) protocol.
The RTC4 provides various analog and digital signal 
outputs freely available for tailoring laser control to 
user-specific requirements. The user assumes 
responsibility for use of these signals. 
4.1.1
List Commands and 
Control Commands
The RTC4 command set contains: 
• Control commands 
• List commands 
Control commands are executed immediately.
They are used for controlling execution of lists and for 
setting some general parameters. Other control 
commands are provided for direct laser and 
scan head control.
Before a list command can be sent to the RTC4, a list 
must be opened by a control command. List 
commands sent to the RTC4 afterwards are not 
executed immediately, but stored in a list buffer. Only 
after the list is closed and started, the RTC4 reads the 
commands from the list buffer and processes them in 
real time. The RTC4 provides two list buffers. Each list 
buffer can hold one list (“list 1“ or “list 2“) with up to 
4000 list commands. If an application needs only one 
list, then the RTC4’s entire memory can be treated like 
a single list with a capacity for 8000 commands. In 
this case, list 1 can be loaded with up to 8000 
commands (but list 2 must not be used). This also 
makes possible the use of structured programming.
List commands include jump[*] Commands, 
mark[*] Commands and arc[*] Commands, as well as 
commands for setting various scanning parameters 
such as laser power, jump speed and marking speed.
Some commands exist in two versions: as a 
list command and as a control command. Among 
these dual-version commands are the I/O commands.
(1) In this manual, laser marking is mentioned only as an 
example of the many possible laser materials 
processing applications.

All RTC4 commands are described in detail in: 
• Chapter 11, “RTC4 Commands”, Page 113 
• Chapter 16.6.2, “Command Set for the 
RTC4 Ethernet Board”, Page 238 
4.1.2
List Handling and Status
set_start_list opens a list buffer for writing. After 
finishing the data input, the list must be closed with 
the list command set_end_of_list.
As soon as a list is closed, it can be started either with 
the control command execute_list or by an external 
start signal, see Chapter 4.1.4, “External Control 
Inputs”, Page 26.
During the execution of one list, the other list buffer 
can be loaded with the next list. The host PC and the 
RTC4 then work in parallel. The second list can only 
be started after execution of the first list has finished. 
During execution of a list, execute_list and external 
start signals are ignored.
Execution of a list can be stopped at any time, for 
example, for implementing an emergency shutdown 
or for any other purpose. Use of the stop_execution 
or an external stop signal immediately aborts the 
currently running list and turns off all RTC4 laser 
signals.
To examine the current status of the lists, get_status 
or read_status can be used.
4.1.3
Automatic List Handling
Continuous Transfer
auto_change and auto_change_pos activate an 
automatic list change. That causes the next sequence 
of commands to start automatically when the current 
list is finished. In this manner, continuous data 
transfer to the scan head can be achieved without 
non-productive pauses. 
auto_change can be called when working with two 
lists (each with a maximum capacity of 4000 
commands). auto_change should only be called 
while a list is executing or after a list has finished. 
Additionally, the next list should have already been 
loaded and closed by set_end_of_list.
auto_change_pos can be called when the entire 
RTC4 memory is to be treated like a single list. The 
start position (address in list memory) of the next 
command sequence is specified by the command.
Alternating Output
Alternatively, continuous data transfer can be 
achieved by alternately repeating output of the two 
lists:
(1) Load and close both lists.
(2) Start one of the lists by execute_list.
(3) While the first list is running, call start_loop. This 
causes an automatic continuous and alternating 
output of both lists from the RTC4 to the 
scan head, until you choose to call quit_loop, 
which terminates the continuous output as soon 
as the current list is finished.
RTC4-Circular Queue Mode 
RTC4-Circular Queue Mode is an additional mode of 
operation with continuous command transfer, see 
Chapter 5.5, “RTC4-Circular Queue Mode”, Page 64.

[p.26]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
4 RTC4 Principle of Operation 
26
innovators for industry
4.1.4
External Control Inputs
To also enable start and stop execution of a list by 
external signals, the RTC4 provides two external 
control inputs, /START and /STOP 
(TTL active-low). These control inputs are accessible 
by the 9-pin D-SUB laser connector VB3 on the RTC4 
board, see Figure 24. Both signal inputs are internally 
connected to +5 V by pull-up resistors (4.7 k).
Both inputs are active-LOW, that is, the 
corresponding pin must be set to the LOW level (0 V) 
to start or to stop execution.
STOP Signal
If the /STOP input is at the LOW level for at least 
10 µs, execution of the currently running list is 
aborted immediately and the RTC4 laser signals are 
turned off. This is equivalent to calling 
stop_execution.
The /STOP input is always enabled. It can, for 
instance, be used to implement an emergency 
shutdown.
START Signal
Before the /START input can be used, it has to be 
enabled by set_control_mode. Subsequent START 
requests start the loaded list. The list is only started 
by the external START request if it is closed and no list 
is executing at the moment.
The desired list can be selected by select_list. 
Alternatively, the list command set_extstartpos_list 
can be used.
To check whether a list was successfully started, 
the command get_startstop_info can be used.
Notes 
• A stop_execution call disables the external 
/START input. An external /STOP signal also (at 
least temporarily) disables the external /start 
input, that is, as long as the /STOP input is LOW. 
set_control_mode defines whether or not the 
/START input also stays disabled when the /STOP 
signal is no longer active.
• Important: 
– The /START input is edge sensitive 
(HIGH to LOW level transition), 
– The /STOP input is level sensitive

[p.27]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
4 RTC4 Principle of Operation 
27
innovators for industry
4.1.5
Synchronization of Processing
set_wait makes it possible to set wait markers (break 
points) within a list. Each marker is associated with a 
number greater than zero.
When the RTC4 reaches a wait marker, processing of 
the list is temporarily interrupted and the laser is 
turned off.
get_wait_status ascertains whether processing is 
currently interrupted at a marker. If processing is 
interrupted, get_wait_status returns the number 
(wait_word) of the corresponding marker. Otherwise 
the command returns zero.
The wait markers are provided for synchronization 
purposes. The user program should perform a 
handling routine for each wait marker.
When that handling routine is finished, processing of 
the list can be resumed by the control command 
release_wait.

[p.28]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
4 RTC4 Principle of Operation 
28
innovators for industry
4.2
Scan Head and Laser Control
4.2.1
Vector Commands
The main commands for scan head control are: 
• jump[*] Commands, Page 28 
• mark[*] Commands, Page 28 
Each of these commands describes a vector. 
The end point of the corresponding vector must be 
specified as a parameter in each case (1). Each vector 
starts at the current output position, which is usually 
the end point of the preceding vector. The initial 
output position at start-up (or after a reset of the 
RTC4) is the center of the Image Field, that is, the 
point (0|0).
See Chapter 4.4, “Image Field Size”, Page 45 for a 
description of the Image Field coordinate system. 
jump[*] Commands
A jump[*] Command (jump_abs or jump_rel) causes 
a (usually) fast motion of the mirrors. The laser focus 
“jumps“ to the new position. In general, the laser is 
switched off during the jump. The jump speed is 
specified by set_jump_speed. 
If the laser system does not allow fast switching, the 
jump speed must be set high enough to prevent a 
visible marking effect on the workpiece. See also 
home_position.
mark[*] Commands
When a mark[*] Command (mark_abs or mark_rel) 
is executed, the laser focus is guided at a constant 
speed from the starting point to the end point of a 
vector and produces a rectilinear mark on the surface 
of the workpiece.
The RTC4 automatically turns on the laser at the 
beginning of the mark[*] Command execution. 
The laser remains on if a mark[*] Command is 
immediately followed by another mark[*] Command 
(or an arc[*] Command). A Polyline can therefore be 
marked by directly concatenating 
mark[*] Commands (mark[*] Command series)). 
The laser is turned off after the last 
mark[*] Command of a Polyline or the end of the 
current list is reached.
The mark speed is set with the list command 
set_mark_speed (usually lower than the 
jump speed). It can be changed at any point within a 
list. 
(1) The coordinates must be specified as digital control 
values (without units). To avoid confusion with 
coordinates in [mm], SCANLAB uses the expression 
“coordinate values [in Bits]”.

[p.29]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
4 RTC4 Principle of Operation 
29
innovators for industry
4.2.2
arc[*] Commands
arc[*] Commands are available for marking circular 
arcs. The coordinates of the arc center and the arc 
angle must be specified as parameters. The arc starts 
at the current output position.
When an arc[*] Command (arc_abs or arc_rel) is 
executed, the laser focus is guided along the specified 
arc at the specified speed. 
The RTC4 automatically turns on the laser at the 
beginning of the arc[*] Command execution. 
The laser is turned off when the last command of a 
arc[*] Command series (or mark[*] Command series) 
or the end of the current list is reached. 
4.2.3
Microstepping
The RTC4 PCIe Board splits up each 
• jump[*] Command 
• mark[*] Command 
• arc[*] Command 
into so-called 
• Microsteps 
(not: Microvectors). 
The split-up of the x component of a vector into 
Microsteps is shown in Figure 4. 
All Microsteps are transferred to the scan head with 
a constant output interval t of 10 µs and cannot be 
changed. 
The following applies:
Length s of a Microstep = v × t 
(v = current jump speed or mark speed)

Notes 
• The RTC4 is a 16-bit board. Therefore, one vector 
or arc can be split up into 216 Microsteps. 
If the mark speed is quite low and the vector or 
arc is very long, a output period of 10 µs would 
possibly lead to more than 216 Microsteps. In this 
case, the RTC4 automatically increases the output 
period to 20 µs (or to a suitable multiple of 10 µs, 
if necessary).
Marking Time
The marking time consumed by any particular 
marking process can be measured by calling the 
command save_and_restart_timer before and after 
the marking process. This command saves the current 
value of the RTC4’s integrated timer and resets the 
timer value to 0. The measured time can be read by 
get_time, which returns the timer value saved during 
the most recent call of save_and_restart_timer.

innovators for industry
4.3
Delay Settings – Coordinating 
Scan Head Control and Laser 
Control
Scan head control and laser control should suit the 
dynamic behavior of the system components, that is, 
the 
• response behavior of the laser 
• response behavior of the galvanometer scanners 
(Tracking Error) 
• the type of interaction between laser radiation 
and material 
The following delays are available for this purpose: 
• Laser Delays 
– LaserOn Delay 
– LaserOff Delay 
• Scanner delays 
– Jump Delay (optionally: variable) 
– Mark Delay 
– Polygon Delay (optionally: variable) 
4.3.1
Laser Delays
There are two different Laser Delays: 
• LaserOn Delay 
• LaserOff Delay 
Laser Delays affect when the laser is turned on or off 
before or after a mark[*] Command or 
arc[*] Command or a series of 
mark[*] Commands and arc[*] Commands. 
Laser Delays do not affect the total marking time, 
except when they are negative.
The LaserOn Delay and the LaserOff Delay are set by 
the list command set_laser_delays. The time 
resolution for the Laser Delays is 1 µs.
The delays must be appropriate for the defined 
jump speed and mark speed, see also Chapter 4.3.3, 
“Notes on Optimizing the Delays”, Page 40.

[p.32]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
4 RTC4 Principle of Operation 
32
innovators for industry
LaserOn Delay
The LaserOn Delay defines the moment when the 
RTC4 turns on the laser. LaserOn Delay is 
automatically inserted at the start of a 
mark[*] Command or arc[*] Command (first 
Microstep). Thus, the laser is switched on only after 
execution of the first few Microsteps. This delay can 
be used for several purposes:
• Many applications require laser marking without 
variations of intensity, especially without burn-in 
effects at the start or end of a vector. To achieve 
homogeneous marking results, it is essential to 
scan the vectors with a constant velocity.
At the beginning of a mark[*] Command or 
arc[*] Command, however, the mirrors first have 
to be accelerated up to the defined mark speed. 
Figure 7 shows that the laser focus initially moves 
only very slowly. A burn-in effect may occur.
To avoid this, the LaserOn Delay must be set to a 
suitable positive value. Thus the mirrors have 
already reached a certain angular velocity when 
the laser eventually turns on. However, if the 
LaserOn Delay is too long, the first part of 
the vector is cut off.
• Some materials take some time until they react to 
the exposure to laser radiation. In this case, it can 
be useful to “preheat“ the starting point of a 
mark or arc vector before marking. This can be 
done by setting the LaserOn Delay to a negative 
value. 
A negative LaserOn Delay extends the total 
marking time, because it is inserted before the 
current mark[*] Command or arc[*] Command.
LaserOff Delay
Due to the acceleration phase at the beginning 
of the motion, a difference (Tracking Error) occurs 
between the set position and the actual position 
of each mirror, see Figure 7.
After execution of a mark[*] Command or 
arc[*] Command, the laser should not be turned off 
immediately because the scanners have not yet 
reached the final set position. Therefore, a 
LaserOff Delay is inserted automatically before the 
laser is turned off.

[p.33]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
4 RTC4 Principle of Operation 
33
innovators for industry
4.3.2
Scanner Delays
There are three different types of Scanner Delays: 
• Jump Delays 
• Mark Delays 
• Polygon Delays 
After each Vector Command or arc[*] Command, the 
RTC4 inserts one of these delays before the next 
command is started.
set_scanner_delays defines the Scanner Delays. 
Scanner Delays are specified in units of 10 µs.
Jump Delay
When executing a jump[*] Command, the mirrors 
first have to be accelerated up to the defined 
jump speed. Because of the inertia of the mirrors, a 
lag called Tracking Error occurs between the 
set position and the actual position, see Figure 5.
At the end of the jump, a certain settling time is 
necessary for the mirrors to reach the set position 
within some accuracy. To allow for the settling time 
and for the Tracking Error, the RTC4 inserts a 
Jump Delay after each jump[*] Command, before the 
next command is executed, see Figure 5. 
The length of the Jump Delay must be defined by the 
list command set_scanner_delays. Note that a 
higher jump speed also requires a longer Jump Delay.
The total time needed for the entire 
jump[*] Command is the sum of the actual jump time 
and the Jump Delay. It can be minimized by 
optimizing the jump speed and the Jump Delay.

Scan head control timing during a jump[*] Command with a constant Jump Delay. 
The laser remains off.

Variable Jump Delays
With short jumps, the scan head often does not reach 
the full jump speed. Then shorter Jump Delays are 
sufficient for settling of the mirrors: 
• “Variable Jump Delays“ 
For this, there is: 
• “Variable Jump Delays“ mode 
“Variable Jump Delays“ mode is switched on by 
JumpLengthLimit > 0 (at set_delay_mode).
Then the RTC6 PCIe Board inserts a linearly 
interpolated Jump Delay between MinJumpDelay (from 
set_delay_mode) and jump_delay (Jump Delay from 
set_scanner_delays) for jump lengths between 0 
and Jump length limit, see Figure 6. 
With jump lengths larger than JumpLengthLimit a 
“full” Jump Delay is always inserted. 
Notes 
• With “Variable Jump Delays“ mode, total 
marking time is reduced, especially when there 
are many short jumps. 
• “Variable Jump Delays“ mode is switched off by 
JumpLengthLimit = 0 (at set_delay_mode). 
• After jumps of length 0, the duration of “Variable 
Jump Delays“ is 0.

Mark Delay
Although the mark speed is usually set lower than 
the jump speed, a Tracking Error also occurs during a 
mark[*] Command or arc[*] Command. 
To make sure that the mirrors reach the final 
set position properly before the next command, the 
RTC4 inserts a Mark Delay, see also Figure 7 and 
Figure 8: 
• After a single mark[*] Command 
• After a single arc[*] Command 
• After the last mark[*] Command of a Polyline 
• After the last arc[*] Command of an 
arc[*] Command series 
Notes 
• If a mark[*] Command or arc[*] Command is 
followed by a zero jump[*] Command, 
mark[*] Command or arc[*] Command, then the 
MarkDelay is not executed.
• If a jump vector is followed by a zero jump vector, 
then the first JumpDelay is not executed.
In contrast, the JumpDelay is executed if the jump 
vector is followed by a zero mark or 
arc[*] Command.

Polygon Delay
Between two successive mark[*] Commands or 
arc[*] Commands, there is no need for a complete 
stop of the mirrors. Therefore, a Polygon Delay is set 
instead of a Mark Delay, see Figure 8. 
The Mark Delay and the Polygon Delay can be set 
independently. In addition, the RTC4 is able to vary 
the length of the Polygon Delay, depending on the 
angle between two marking vectors or the tangents 
of the arcs. See Section ”Variable Polygon Delays”, 
Page 37.

Variable Polygon Delays
The RTC4 can carry out a user-defined variation of the 
Polygon Delay length depending on the size of the 
angle  between two successive mark vectors, 
see Figure 9. 
For this, it must be enabled by set_delay_mode: 
• “Variable Polygon Delays” mode 
For each corner of the Polyline, the RTC4 calculates 
the “Variable Polygon Delay” v_delay() as follows:
v_delay() = scale() × polygon_delay 
scale() is a scaling function (0 scale() 2). 
polygon_delay is a parameter of set_scanner_delays.
Figure 9 shows the default scaling function. 
This standard curve can be replaced by a customized 
curve, see Section ”User-defined “Variable 
Polygon Delays””, Page 39.

edgelevel
The “Variable Polygon Delay” becomes quite long 
with angles  close to 180°, see Figure 9. 
This might lead to burn-in effects in the sharp corners 
of the Polyline. To avoid this, users can define a 
so-called edgelevel: If the Polygon Delay between 
two mark[*] Commands or arc[*] Commands is 
longer than or equal to this value, the RTC4 turns the 
laser off after the first mark[*] Command or 
arc[*] Command – after inserting a LaserOff Delay – 
and starts a new Polyline at the beginning of the next 
mark[*] Command (or arc[*] Command). See also 
Figure 10.
For further details, see set_delay_mode.

User-defined “Variable Polygon Delays”
load_varpolydelay(FileName, X) loads a table for the 
scaling function scale() from an ASCII text file with 
the file extension *.STB. 
An *.STB file can contain one or more tables, 
see load_varpolydelay. 
The table contains up to 50 data points (scale()) 
for various angles . The RTC4 determines the scaling 
function scale() from this data by linear 
interpolation. As an example, Figure 11 shows a table 
with four data points and the corresponding scaling 
function. 
Table Format 
Each table starts with the instruction [VarPolyTableX] 
where X must be replaced by a positive integer which 
denotes the table number. Each data point 
(scale()) is described by two instructions: 
AngleY=… 
ScaleY=…  
where Y must be replaced by an integer (1 Y 50) 
which denotes the number of the data point. The 
values for the angle  (in degrees) and the 
scaling factor must be specified in 64-bit IEEE 
floating point format. Use the period (.) as the 
decimal point. The allowed value ranges are 
0° 180° and 0 scale() 2. 
Notes 
• Each instruction must be in a separate line.
• Empty lines are ignored. 
• All characters following a semicolon (;) are 
ignored until the end of the line, that is, the 
semicolon can be used for comments. 
• The order of the data points in the table is of no 
importance. 
• The angle = 0° means that two successive 
vectors are parallel and are marked in the same 
direction. 
If the table contains no explicit data for = 0°, 
the scaling factor scale(0°) = 0 is assumed. 
• The angle = 180° means that two successive 
vectors are marked in the opposite direction. 
If the table contains no explicit data for = 180°, 
the scaling factor scale(180°) is set to the largest 
scaling factor found in the table.

4.3.3
Notes on Optimizing the 
Delays
The delays are set by set_scanner_delays and 
set_laser_delays. The delays should be appropriate 
for the defined jump speed and the mark speed. If 
the delays are not optimized, the quality of the 
scanning results are possibly insufficient and 
scanning time is extended.
The figures on page 42 through page 44 show the 
various effects of non-optimized delays on the letters 
“RTC“.
Recommended Sequence
(1) The LaserOn Delay and the LaserOff Delay should 
be optimized first. In doing so it is recommended 
to set Jump Delays and Mark Delays to high 
values. 
The lengths of LaserOn Delays and the 
LaserOff Delays have no influence on the total 
scanning time, if positive values are chosen. 
(2) Once the Laser Delays have been set the 
Scanner DelaysScanner Delays (Jump Delay, 
Mark Delay, Polygon Delay) can be optimized. 
Limits for the Delays
When setting the delays, observe the following:
(1) The LaserOff Delay must be longer than the 
LaserOn Delay. Otherwise faults in the laser 
control may occur. ‘
LaserOff Delay > LaserOn Delay 
(2) The Mark Delay and the Jump Delay must be 
longer than the difference between the 
LaserOff Delay and the LaserOn Delay. 
Mark Delay > LaserOff Delay – LaserOn Delay 
Jump Delay > LaserOff Delay – LaserOn Delay 
The same applies to the edgelevel value of the 
“Variable Polygon Delay”: 
edgelevel > LaserOff Delay – LaserOn Delay 
(3) Under certain circumstances the Polygon Delay 
must be longer than the difference between 
LaserOff Delay and LaserOn Delay, see 
Explanations, Page 41. 
Polygon Delay > LaserOff Delay – LaserOn Delay 
Note that the Laser Delays must be specified in 
units of 1 µs, whereas the Scanner Delays 
(Jump Delay, Mark Delay, Polygon Delay) must be 
specified in units of 10 µs.

[p.41]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
4 RTC4 Principle of Operation 
41
innovators for industry
Explanations 
These conditions can be explained as follows:
(1) Consider a very short mark command. 
If the sum of the marking time and the 
LaserOff Delay is shorter than the LaserOn Delay, 
the 
LaserOff Delay is over before the LaserOn Delay 
has finished. 
Thus the RTC4 first tries to turn the laser off and 
afterwards turn it on:
This can be avoided by always setting the 
LaserOff Delay longer than the LaserOn Delay. 
LaserOff Delay > LaserOn Delay
[1]
(2) Consider two subsequent mark commands. 
If the LaserOff Delay (after the first 
mark command) is longer than the sum of the 
Mark Delay and the LaserOn Delay (for the 
second mark command), the RTC4 turns off the 
laser during the second mark command:
To avoid this, the sum of the Mark Delay and the 
LaserOn Delay must be longer than the 
LaserOff Delay. 
Mark Delay + LaserOn Delay > LaserOff Delay [2]
In practice, the Laser Delays are usually optimized 
first. 
Then the following condition applies to the 
Mark Delay:
Mark Delay > LaserOff Delay – LaserOn Delay [3]
that is, the Mark Delay must be longer than the 
difference between the LaserOff Delay and the 
LaserOn Delay.
(3) If a list change is necessary while a Polyline is 
executed, but not auto_change is used for this 
purpose and the second list is started 
immediately after complete execution of the first 
list, then the same as for the Mark Delay is 
required for the Polygon Delay:
Polygon Delay + LaserOn Delay > LaserOff Delay[4]
Otherwise, it could occur that the laser is off for 
the rest of the Polyline.
SCANLAB recommends to use the auto_change 
command for automatic list change or to add a 
small software delay of the size 
(LOffD - LOnD - polygonD) previously to starting 
the second list.

Potential Errors when Optimizing the Delays
The following figures show the various effects of 
unsuitable delays on the marking result, in this 
example on the lettering “RTC“.  
LaserOn Delay too short
At the beginning of a mark 
vector the laser is switched on, 
even though the mirrors have 
not yet reached the necessary 
angular velocity. 
Burn-in effects at the start points 
of the respective vectors result. 
LaserOn Delay too long 
The laser is switched on too late 
at the beginning of a mark 
vector. 
The first part of the vector is not 
marked.

The laser is switched off after a 
mark[*] Command before the 
mirrors have reached the vector 
end position. 
The respective vectors are not 
marked completely. 
LaserOff Delay too long 
The laser is switched off too late 
after a mark[*] Command. The 
laser is still on, although the 
mirrors are already slowed down 
considerably or have already 
stopped.
The results are burn-in effects at 
the end points of the respective 
vectors.

4.4
Image Field Size
Figure 12 shows the reference system for the Image 
Field which is used by the RTC4. The y axis points 
in the reverse direction of the input laser beam, the 
x axis points to the right of the y axis. x axis, y axis 
and z axis (optional) form a right-handed reference 
system. The origin of the reference system, that is, the 
point (0|0|0), is in the center of the Image Field. 
The size of the usable Image Field is determined by 
the maximum scan angle and the focal length of the 
objective or the working distance (that is, the 
distance between the input laser beam axis and the 
Image Field).
All X and Y vector coordinates must be specified as 
signed 16-bit numbers (that is, as numbers between 
–32768 and +32767). 
The ratio of a point coordinate in bits(1) and the 
actual position of the point in millimeters is defined 
by the calibration factor K(2). 
Let a0 denote the side length of the Image Field given 
by the maximum scan angle. The theoretical 
calibration factor is then K0 = 216/a0  [bits per 
mm(1)].
SCANLAB provides a rounded value for the 
calibration factor K. This value is slightly larger than, 
but close to, the theoretical value. 
The actual calibration factor K can be found in the 
*_ReadMe.txt file that accompanies the 
correction file(3). 
Given the calibration factor K, the side length in 
millimeters a of the usable Image Field can be 
calculated: 
a = 216 / K 
12

(0|0|0)

(1) The expression “bits” is here synonymous with “digital 
control value”, see footnote on page 28.
(2) The calibration factor is the same for the X and Y 
direction and – for 3D scan systems – also for the Z 
direction. 
(3) Only in the scope of delivery of special system 
configurations.

innovators for industry
4.4.1
Typical Image Field
In general, the objective and the optical 
configuration of the scan head further reduce the 
size of the usable Image Field. 
The scan head has a “typical Image Field“. If the laser 
focus moves outside this typical Image Field, some 
vignetting of the laser beam can occur. The interior 
of the scan head can be damaged due to excessive 
absorption of laser power. Observe the 
corresponding sections in the manual of your 
scan head. 
(1) Compare the calculated side length a (as 
described above) with the side length b of the 
typical Image Field given in the technical 
specifications of your scan head manual. 
(2) If the laser focus shall be restricted to points 
within the typical Image Field, the absolute values 
of the X and Y coordinates (in bits) must be 
smaller than the maximum value M, where M 
is the calibration factor K multiplied by half the 
side length of the typical Image Field: 
M = K × b/2.
4.5
Image Field Correction
The deflection of a laser beam with a two-mirror 
system results in three effects: 
(1) The arrangement of the mirrors leads to a 
distortion of the Image Field, see Figure 14. 
This distortion arises from the fact that 
the distance between mirror 1 and the Image 
Field depends on the size of the scan angles of 
mirror 1 and mirror 2. A larger scan angle leads to 
a longer distance. 
(2) The distance in the Image Field is not 
proportional to the scan angle itself, but to the 
tangent of the scan angle. Therefore, the 
mark speed of the laser focus in the Image Field is 
not proportional to the angular velocity of the 
corresponding scanner. 
(3) If an ordinary lens is used for focusing the laser 
beam, the focus lies on a sphere. In a flat Image 
Field plane, a varying spot size results.

4.5.1
F-Theta Objective
By focusing the deflected laser beam with an F-Theta 
objective, two of the three effects can be avoided:
• A direct proportionality between scan angle and 
distance in the Image Field is obtained.
• Additionally, the focus lies on a flat surface.
However, the F-Theta objective causes a barrel-
shaped distortion of the Image Field. This distortion 
is superimposed on the pillow-shaped distortion 
caused by the arrangement of the mirrors, resulting 
in a barrel-pillow distortion of the Image Field, see 
Figure 15. 
4.5.2
Image Field Correction 
Algorithm
The RTC4 board provides a correction algorithm to 
compensate for the Image Field distortion. The 
algorithm is based on a correction table.
An orthogonal grid of 65 × 65 points is 
superimposed on the ideal square Image Field. The 
adjusted X and Y coordinates for the correct output 
of these grid points are stored in a correction table. 
To move the focus to any point within the Image 
Field, the RTC4 calculates the correct coordinates by 
interpolating from the grid points in the 
correction table. The result is transmitted to the 
scan head. The correction algorithm is executed for 
every single Microstep, see also Chapter 4.2.3, 
“Microstepping”, Page 29.
By default, SCANLAB creates an individual 
correction table for every delivered system. It is 
stored in a file named *.CTB in the RTC4 software 
package. See also load_correction_file. 
The calculation of the correction table is based solely 
on general system data (such as mirror geometry, 
calibration and the objective specifications). 
Standard correction files therefore reflect neither the 
unique properties possessed by the customer’s 
individual system, nor alignment errors.
For those customers requiring more accurate 
correction files tailored to the unique properties of 
their particular scan systems, SCANLAB offers the 
correXion program line. These generate 
correction files based on test measurement data. For 
further information, refer to the corresponding 
manual or contact SCANLAB).

and by the F-Theta objective.

4.6
Laser Control
The RTC4 provides several laser control signals 
with programmable pulse width and frequency. 
These signals can be used for controlling various 
types of lasers.
By set_laser_mode, users can set 1 out of 5 different 
laser modes: 
• CO2 Mode (designed for controlling a CO2 laser)
• YAG Mode 1, 2, 3 (three variants designed for 
controlling Nd:YAG and related lasers)
• Laser Mode 4 generates a continuously-running 
control signal.
Depending on the selected laser mode, different 
laser control signals are available. These signals are 
described in the following. 
Notes 
• set_laser_mode must be called during initializing 
the RTC4 for selecting the laser mode. After the 
initialization the laser control is activated. During 
operation the selected laser signals can be 
deactivated by the control command 
disable_laser and again reactivated by the 
control command enable_laser. 
get_startstop_info (Bit #9) provides 
information about the current status of the laser 
control.
• All laser signals are TTL signals. They can be set to 
either active-high or active-low with a jumper, see 
Chapter 9.1.1, “TTL Laser Signal Level”, Page 96.
The maximum current load for all laser signals is 
10 mA. If jumper X6 has been configured to 
replace the ANALOG OUT 1 signal with +5 V, then 
the maximum current load at pin (4) of the laser 
connector is 100 mA.
The signals are available by the Laser Connector, 
Page 84. 
4.6.1
CO2 Mode
set_laser_mode(0) sets the CO2 Mode. 
In CO2 Mode, the following laser signals are 
available, see Figure 16:
• a LASERON signal
• two alternating modulation signals with variable 
pulse width and frequency (LASER1 and LASER2)
The signals are available by the 9-pin D-SUB 
Laser Connector, Page 84. The LASER1 signals and 
LASER2 signals are additionally available at the 
LASER EXTENSION socket connector, see page 86.
The signals LASER1 and LASER2 have a constant 
relative phase shift of 180° (half an output period). 
LASER2 can be used for the control of a second laser 
tube.
To control the laser power, the pulse lengths of both 
laser signals can be varied independently. However, 
the output frequency of both signals is the same.
The output frequency and the pulse lengths have to 
be specified with the list command 
set_laser_timing. Note that half of the output 
period must be specified, that is, the shift between 
the two laser signals. 
set_laser_timing is a list command, that is, it can be 
used anywhere in a list. This allows, for example, 
changing the laser power at any time within a list.
Notes 
• The actual output period and the pulse lengths of 
laser signals LASER1 and LASER2 depend on the 
clock frequency, which has to be specified by 
set_laser_timing. The clock frequency can be set 
to 1 MHz or 8 MHz.
• In general, it is recommended to set the 
clock frequency to 8 MHz (by 
set_laser_timing(…,…,…,1)). 
A clock frequency of 1 MHz should only be 
chosen if necessary.

[p.49]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
4 RTC4 Principle of Operation 
49
innovators for industry
Stand-By Mode
While the LASERON signal is inactive, the output 
of signals LASER1 and LASER2 is reduced to stand-by 
pulses. The pulse length and the output frequency of 
these stand-by pulses are set by set_standby. They 
are equal for both laser signals.
The stand-by pulses can be turned off by setting the 
stand-by pulse length to zero (default setting).

4.6.2
YAG Mode 1, 2, 3
By set_laser_mode(1), set_laser_mode(2) or 
set_laser_mode(3) one of the three available YAG 
modes is set. 
In all three YAG modes, the RTC4 supplies the 
following signals:
• a LASERON signal
• a Q-Switch signal with variable pulse width and 
frequency (LASER1)
• a programmable FirstPulseKiller signal (LASER2)
The signals are available by the 9-pin D-SUB 
Laser Connector, Page 84. The LASER1 signals and 
LASER2 signals are additionally available at the 
LASER EXTENSION socket connector, see page 86.
Q-Switch Signal
The Q-Switch signal serves to control the quality 
switch. The Q-Switch period and pulse length are set 
by set_laser_timing. Note that half of the output 
period must be specified. The parameter 
pulse_width2 (for the LASER2 signal) has no effect in 
the YAG modes.
Notes 
• Note that the actual Q-Switch period and the 
pulse length depend on the clock frequency 
specified by set_laser_timing. 
The clock frequency can be set to 1 MHz (1 bit 
equals 1 µs) or 8 MHz (1 bit equals 1/8 µs).
• In general, it is recommended to set the 
clock frequency to 8 MHz with 
set_laser_timing(…,…,…,1). A clock frequency of 
1 MHz should only be set, if necessary.
FirstPulseKiller Signal
The FirstPulseKiller signal is available for reduction of 
the laser pulse power at the beginning of a pulse 
train. The initial pulses of a pulse train often have a 
higher energy than the following pulses. These 
intensity variations are to be avoided in most laser 
marking applications. Therefore, the laser should be 
equipped with a FirstPulseKiller device that reduces 
the power of the first laser pulses.
The RTC4 provides a control signal for the 
FirstPulseKiller (TTL level). The FirstPulseKiller signal is 
started together with the LASERON signal. While the 
FirstPulseKiller signal is active, the energy of the 
pulses should be reduced adequately.
The length of the FirstPulseKiller signal is set by 
set_firstpulse_killer or set_firstpulse_killer_list. 
Differences Between YAG Modes
The three YAG laser modes only differ in the relative 
start time of the first Q-Switch pulse, see also the 
timing diagrams in Figure 17.
• YAG Mode 1 
The first Q-Switch pulse starts at the beginning of 
the FirstPulseKiller signal. 
• YAG Mode 2 
The first Q-Switch pulse starts at the end of the 
FirstPulseKiller signal. This mode is provided for 
certain YAG laser types. 
• YAG Mode 3 
The first Q-Switch pulse starts 10 µs after the 
beginning of the FirstPulseKiller signal. 
Lamp Current (Laser Power)
To control the lamp current of a YAG laser, the analog 
output signal ANALOG OUT1 (10-bit signal) can be 
used. This signal is available by the 9-pin D-SUB laser 
connector, see Figure 24. To set the output signal, use 
write_da_1 or write_da_1_list.
Alternatively, the lamp current can be controlled 
digitally by the 8-bit digital output port on the 
LASER EXTENSION socket connector, see Figure 25. 
The commands for setting the 8-bit port are 
write_8bit_port and write_8bit_port_list.
See also “LASER EXTENSION Socket Connector”, 
Page 86.

4.6.3
Laser Mode 4
set_laser_mode(4) sets Laser Mode 4. 
In Laser Mode 4, the following laser signals are 
available, see Figure 18:
• a LASERON signal
• a continuously-running modulation signal with 
variable pulse width and frequency (LASER1). The 
signal is provided for an active LASERON signal as 
well as for an inactive LASERON signal (as 
standby signal).
• a programmable FirstPulseKiller signal 
(LASER2)
The signals are available by the 9-pin D-SUB 
Laser Connector, Page 84. The LASER1 signals and 
LASER2 signals are additionally available at the 
LASER EXTENSION socket connector, see page 86.
The pulse length and the output period of the LASER1 
modulation signal are set by set_standby or 
set_standby_list. In the default setting the pulse 
length is set to zero. Note that half of the output 
period must be specified. The maximum allowed 
pulse length is the half stand-by period.
The FirstPulseKiller signal is started together with the 
LASERON signal. The length of the FirstPulseKiller 
signal is set by set_firstpulse_killer or 
set_firstpulse_killer_list. 
In Laser Mode 4, the clock frequency for the signals 
LASER1 and LASER2 is always 1/8 µs. 
Notes 
• Laser Mode 4 is used for some fiber lasers.

4.6.4
Softstart Mode
For some applications it is important to control the 
laser intensity at the beginning of a marking process.
SCANLAB provides a convenient solution in the form 
of the Softstart Mode, which is available in 
all laser modes. 
In the Softstart Mode, various control values 
(level(0)…level(number), number 1000) for as many 
as the first 1001 pulses (at the beginning of a 
marking process) can be defined. Analog voltage 
levels for variable laser power can be defined, which 
are output either at the ANALOG OUT1 or 
ANALOG OUT2 output ports, see Figure 24.
Initialization of the Softstart Mode is performed by 
set_softstart_mode. Here, the desired analog 
output port can be defined. Afterwards the individual 
softstart control values can be defined by 
set_softstart_level.
After the laser control signals are switched on, the 
defined softstart values (max. 1001) are put out at 
the selected output port simultaneously with the 
laser pulses of the LASER1 signal (selectable with 
either the leading or trailing edges of the laser 
pulses). set_softstart_mode allows specification of a 
time period (restartdelay) for which the laser must 
have been switched off before softstart is activated at 
the next switch-on. 
Notes 
• With a large enough value for the restartdelay 
time, one can avoid the Softstart Mode being also 
activated after very short jump[*] Commands. 
• As soon as the LASERON signal (the laser) remains 
switched off longer than restartdelay, the value 
level(0) is assigned to the output port, provided 
the Softstart Mode has not been meanwhile 
deactivated.
• When the LASERON signal is switched on, then 
the values level(0) …. level(number) are 
automatically assigned to the output port 
simultaneously with the first laser pulses. The 
power of the first laser pulse is determined by 
level(1) if the values are assigned with the 
leading edge of the laser pulse selected, or by 
level(0) if the trailing edge of the laser pulse is 
selected.
• If Softstart Mode is inactive, then values for the 
analog outputs can be set by write_da_x or 
write_da_x_list.
• The Softstart Mode is also available in 
Laser Mode 4. In Laser Mode 4, the LASER1 
output only puts out standby pulses. Here, too, 
the defined softstart values are output 
simultaneously with the pulses of an internally-
generated (but not output) LASER1 signal. The 
standby pulses are not synchronized with the 
internal LASER1 pulses. If the period durations of 
the standby and LASER1 pulses are set (by 
set_laser_timing and set_standby) to be equal, 
then the analog voltage softstart values are put 
out in parallel with the standby pulses, though 
they might have a (random) constant phase shift 
with respect to the standby pulses.
• For the RTC4 board, no pulsewidth softstart 
values (for the laser signal at the LASER1 output) 
can be defined in the Softstart Mode. This 
functionality is additionally provided by the RTC3 
and RTC5 board.

[p.54]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
4 RTC4 Principle of Operation 
54
innovators for industry
4.7
Status Monitoring and 
Diagnostics
For status monitoring and diagnostic purposes, the 
command get_value can be used to read a variety of 
signals:
• XY2-100-compliant (or XY2-100 Enhanced-
compliant status) signals returned by the 
scan system
– The XY2-100 status word can be queried by the 
get_head_status command, too.
– As specified by the XY2-100 Enhanced Protocol, 
iDRIVE scan systems (intelliSCAN, 
intelliSCANde, intelliDRILL, intellicube, 
intelliWELD, varioSCANde) allow various data 
signals for each axis to be transmitted 
separately to the RTC4 for evaluation, see 
Chapter 4.8, “iDRIVE Functions”, Page 55.
• The current value of the LASERON signal.
• The current Cartesian output values (Coordinate 
Transformations defined by set_matrix, 
set_matrix_list, set_offset or set_offset_list 
have been already applied).
• The actual (digital) output values currently being 
transmitted from the RTC4 to the scan system 
(Image Field correction has been already applied 
to these values in accordance with the loaded 
correction file).
set_trigger can be used on each of these signals to 
record their values over time – and with a selectable 
sample period. The recorded values are stored on the 
RTC4. get_waveform can be used to transfer the 
recorded values to the PC.
The current status of a measurement session started 
with set_trigger can be obtained by calling the 
command measurement_status.

[p.55]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
4 RTC4 Principle of Operation 
55
innovators for industry
4.8
iDRIVE Functions
SCANLAB iDRIVE scan systems(1) utilize the iDRIVE 
technology. This new servo and control approach 
exploits the advantages of fully digital servo 
electronics to deliver significantly expanded 
functionality. An enhanced transfer protocol 
between the servo electronics and the 
RTC-control board facilitates support of all the new 
features. See also Chapter 4.8.1, “Enhanced Transfer 
Protocol”, Page 55.
This allows users to adjust a number of settings of the 
respective scan system, for example,
• To select which data it has to transmit to the 
RTC-control board 
• To choose from different dynamic settings 
(tunings) 
• To set the PosAck limit value 
• To set the effective calibration of the scan system 
• To set the start behavior of the scan system 
• To perform a fault diagnosis 
• To perform a functional test of the data transfer 
For more information, refer to the manual of the 
scan system. 
iDRIVE functions are executed by 
control_command. 
4.8.1
Enhanced Transfer Protocol
To accommodate full functionality of the iDRIVE 
technology, data transfer to iDRIVE scan systems is 
realized by an enhanced transfer protocol. This 
allows, for instance, separate, simultaneous 
evaluation of the X and Y status signals (and also – 
when required – separate evaluation of the z axis 
status signals). 
The enhanced protocol is downward-compatible 
with the conventional XY2-100 Protocol. Thus, 
iDRIVE scan systems can also be operated by all 
SCANLAB RTC boards to thereby obtain the standard 
functionality typical of traditional SCANLAB 
scan systems.
Achieving the full iDRIVE functionality, though, 
requires use of the enhanced transfer protocol and an 
RTC4 or newer.
(1) See Glossary entry on page 14.

[p.56]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
4 RTC4 Principle of Operation 
56
innovators for industry
4.8.2
Configuring the Data Signal 
Transmission Behavior of the 
Scan System
Selecting Data Signals
The digital servo architecture of iDRIVE 
scan systems(1) allows a wide variety of data signals 
to be returned from the scan system to the 
RTC-control board. 
Each axis has its own status channel on which data is 
transmitted to the RTC-control board every 10 µs: 
• STATUS channel 
– Designed for the x axis 
(Galvanometer scanner 2) 
• STATUS1 channel 
– Designed for the y axis 
(Galvanometer scanner 1) 
This opens up possibilities such as monitoring the 
galvanometer scanners’ actual values during 
execution of an application or carrying out 
comprehensive troubleshooting in case of 
operational malfunction.
By control_command( data = 05nnH ), it can be set 
which data the scan system has to transmit to the 
control board. The available data signals are 
described in detail in the manual of the respective 
scan system and (in parts) in the command reference 
of control_command. The selected data source is 
transmitted until another source is selected. 
After every power-up or reset (starting with approx. 
five seconds after the power-up or reset), the 
scan system transmits (on all receive channels) the 
XY2-100 status word. 
Reading Out Data
Data received by the RTC4 can be read 
asynchronously at any time by the commands 
get_value or get_head_status or synchronously by 
set_trigger, see Chapter 4.7, “Status Monitoring and 
Diagnostics”, Page 54 and the corresponding 
command references. Note that switching of the data 
source is followed by a short (serial transmission-
related) delay before the first data is transmitted. 
Therefore, after switching data sources you should 
wait at least 50 µs before reading.
The value ranges of transmitted data and the possible 
status states are described in Chapter 18, 
“Appendix C: iDRIVE Scan Systems - Control 
Commands and Signals Transmitted to RTC Control 
Boards”, Page 273. 
Notes 
• get_head_status can only be used if the 
XY2-100 status word is selected to be 
transmitted by the scan system.
(1) See Glossary entry on page 14.

[p.57]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
4 RTC4 Principle of Operation 
57
innovators for industry
4.8.3
Selecting the Tuning 
(Dynamics Setting) 
SCANLAB can optimize the dynamics setting of 
scan systems (tuning) to accommodate differing 
requirements of diverse applications regarding the 
laser positioning dynamics, for example: 
• Vector tuning 
to execute vectors or circular arcs at a constant 
processing speed 
• Jump tuning 
to execute jumps of minimized duration 
iDRIVE scan systems(1) can be optionally equipped 
with several tunings. For different applications, the 
suitable tuning can be switched to – separately for 
each axis – by control_command( data = 11nnH ). 
The default start behavior is for the scan system to 
start with its first tuning (number 0) upon power-up 
or after a reset.
4.8.4
Configuring the PosAck Limit 
Value
control_command( data = 15nnH ) can be used to 
set the PosAck limit value. 
The default start behavior is for the scan system to set 
the limit value to 0.28% of the full position range 
(that is, 0.28% of 216 counts) after every power-up or 
reset. If other limit values are desired, they must be 
separately set for each axis. 
4.8.5
Configuring the Effective 
Calibration
The servo electronic can also be configured by 
control_command( data = 12nnH ) to scale the 
position values received from the RTC4 by a specific 
factor (1, 1/2, 1/4 or 1/8). 
The position signals (optionally) returned by the 
scan systems to the RTC4 remain unaffected, as do 
the preconfigured calibrations of SCANLAB’s 
scan systems. However, the effective calibration can 
be thereby reduced to confine the scan area to a 
smaller angular range – with a higher angular 
resolution.
The default start behavior is for the scan system to 
start with a scaling factor of 1 (that is, with 
SCANLAB’s preconfigured calibration) upon power-
up or after a reset.
By control_command( data = 053FH ), the currently 
set scaling factor can be read out. 
(1) See Glossary entry on page 14.

[p.58]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
4 RTC4 Principle of Operation 
58
innovators for industry
4.8.6
Configuring the Start Behavior
In their default configuration the iDRIVE scan systems 
are preconfigured by SCANLAB so that
• the first tuning (number 0) is selected, see also 
Chapter 4.8.3, “Selecting the Tuning (Dynamics 
Setting)”, Page 57,
• a PosAck limit value of 0.28% of the full position 
range (that is, 0.28% of 216 counts) is set, see also 
Chapter 4.8.4, “Configuring the PosAck Limit 
Value”, Page 57),
• a scaling factor of 1 is set, see also Chapter 4.8.5, 
“Configuring the Effective Calibration”, Page 57.
The settings can be changed by control_command. 
The changed settings are only temporary, however 
they can be additionally saved as starting settings for 
subsequent power-ups or resets (power supply 
switched off and switched on) with 
control_command( data = 0A00H ).
As long as the start behavior is not changed as 
described, the scan system starts with the starting 
settings preconfigured by SCANLAB on every power-
up or reset.
The transmission behavior of the scan system can 
only be temporarily changed, see also Chapter 4.8.2, 
“Configuring the Data Signal Transmission Behavior 
of the Scan System”, Page 56. The corresponding 
start behavior ist fixed by SCANLAB: after every 
restart, the scan system transmits the 
XY2-100 status word.
4.8.7
Fault Diagnosis and Functional 
Test
If a problem occurs, the versatile status return 
functions of the iDRIVE scan systems can be used for 
scan system diagnosis, too. These functions allow to 
read for instance
• the current operating state
• the operating state at the moment of the most 
recently occurred operation interruption and
• an event code, indicating which particular event 
caused the scan head to enter a temporary or 
permanent error state.
To verify that data transfer capability between the 
RTC4 and scan system is intact, control_command 
can transmit an 8-bit value – separately for each axis– 
to the scan system. Subsequently, a 16-bit value is 
returned on the corresponding status channel: If data 
transfer is error-free, then the upper 8 bits of the 
returned 16-bit value is identical with the originally 
sent 8-bit value, and the lower 8 bits are identical 
with the complement of the sent 8-bit value.
These 16-bit values are returned until the 
control_command is used to select another return 
data type, see Chapter 4.8.2, “Configuring the Data 
Signal Transmission Behavior of the Scan System”, 
Page 56.
To facilitate – after a data transfer verification – 
restoration of the behavior in effect prior to the data 
transfer verification, the control_command allows 
the prior data type to be temporarily stored for later 
retrieval.

5
Advanced Programming
5.1
Coordinate Transformations
The RTC4 has the ability to transform the output 
coordinates by the following linear transformation 
for each vector defined by jump[*] Commands, 
mark[*] Commands or arc[*] Commands: 
The coefficients m11…m22 of the (2 x 2) 
transformation matrix are defined by set_matrix. The 
offset (xo|yo) in X and Y direction is defined by 
set_offset. 
Any previously defined matrix and offset definitions 
are thereby overwritten.
The list commands set_matrix_list and 
set_offset_list work in a similar way, see command 
descriptions.
The coordinate transformation defined with these 
commands is applied to the original coordinates of all 
specified vectors (that is, before Microstepping and 
before Image Field correction). 
The transformed vectors are scanned with the correct 
mark speed, even if the length of a vector has 
changed during the transformation.
In a double scan head configuration, the coordinate 
transformation defined with these commands 
applies to both scan heads(1) in the same way.
The transformation matrix can be used for scaling, 
rotating, flipping or skewing an object, see 
Chapter 5.1.1, “Examples”, Page 59. 
To combine several transformations, the 
corresponding matrices are multiplied (in the correct 
order). The resulting matrix is then used in 
set_matrix. 
5.1.1
Examples 
(1) Rotation by the angle : 
For instance, 
set_matrix(0.5, -0.866, 0.866, 0.5) 
defines a rotation by 60° (counterclockwise).
(2) Scaling by the factors kX and kY: 
(3) Flipping in the X direction (mirroring): 
(4) Flipping in the Y direction: 
(5) Exchanging the X and Y axes: 
(6) Skewing in the X direction by the angle  
(slanting): 
Example: set_matrix(1, -0.25, 0, 1) 
(1) See also load_correction_file.

innovators for industry
5.2
Wobbel Mode
Wobbel Mode allows varying the line width during 
laser marking.
Basically, a circular motion is added to the regular, 
linear motion of the output position, resulting in a 
spiral motion of the laser focus in the Image Field. 
A broadening of the original line is obtained by 
choosing suitable values for the amplitude and the 
frequency of the wobbel motion.
For optimum marking results, the wobbel frequency 
should be appropriate for the specified mark speed. 
It may also be necessary to readjust the mark speed. 
set_wobbel sets the wobbel phase to a defined 
starting value (which is independent of the direction 
of the marking vector). 
For further details, see set_wobbel. 
5.2.1
Wobbel Shapes – Important 
Notes on Choosing 
Appropriate Parameter Values 
The RTC4 only supports circles as a wobbel shape. 
These are defined by set_wobbel. Only assign 
hardware appropriate values to the amplitude and 
frequency parameters. 
Rule of thumb to estimate appropriate maximum 
values:
(1) Maximum frequency: F = 1/(10 T) 
where T = Tracking Error(1)
(2) Wobbel amplitude(2): A = T V 
where V = maximum positioning speed(1)
19
Operating principle of the Wobbel Mode. 
Caution!
• Too big values define control situations where 
very high waste heat is produced. Galvanometer 
and digital control board/amplifier board 
overheating and permanent damage may occur 
even in short-term operation (overload!). 
Take the highest possible dynamics of scan head 
and laser into account. 
• If the frequency values are too high the 
galvanometers may not be able to follow the 
nominal curve. This may lead to unexpected 
marking results. 
(1) See technical specifications in the scan head manual
(2) At the maximum frequency estimated with (1)

61
innovators for industry
Notes 
• Also take the path velocity on the wobbel shape 
itself into account. For circular wobbel shapes it is 
calculated as follows:
(3) Path velocity VPath = 2 A F. 
• Make sure that the combination of the used 
values and the Trajectory velocity are suitable for 
a long-term operation without causing damages 
(process safety!).
• Check the temperature status already during an 
evaluation period, see get_head_status, in order 
to recognize potential overload situations at an 
early stage. 
With iDRIVE systems you can also read-out the 
present temperatures of galvanometers and/or 
digital control boards, see control_command.
