innovators for industry
5.3
Using Two Different 
Correction Files 
5.3.1
Double Scan Head 
Configuration
The RTC4 2D version can store two different 
correction files at the same time. For two scan heads 
controlled from a single RTC4 board, each scan head 
can thereby receive its own separate Image Field 
correction.
select_cor_table assigns the both correction files to 
the corresponding scan head control ports, see 
Chapter 6.4, “2. SCANHEAD Socket Connector”, 
Page 87. 
The following steps describe how to use two 
correction files in a double scan head configuration:
(1) Load the first correction file by 
load_correction_file(FileName1, 1, …).
(2) Load the second correction file by 
load_correction_file(FileName2, 2, …).
Specify additional gain, rotation and offset 
to align the two Image Fields precisely with 
respect to each other. Refer to 
load_correction_file for details.
(3) Afterwards, you have to assign the first correction 
file to the first scan head and the second 
correction file to the second scan head by 
select_cor_table(1,2).
Notes 
• The default setting for select_cor_table is (1,0), 
that is, correction table #1 is used for the first 
scan head. The output signals for the second 
scan head are turned off.
• The RTC4 returns to this setting after every reset 
(by one of the commands load_program_file or 
dsp_start).
If a different setting is to be used, the command 
select_cor_table must be called again after the 
reset.
5.3.2
Using Two Correction Files in a 
Single Scan Head System
It can also be useful to work with two different 
correction files in a single scan head system, for 
example if a pointer laser and a main laser with a 
different wavelength are used.
(1) Load the two correction files as described in 
Section “Double Scan Head Configuration”, 
Page 62, left.
(2) Afterwards, use the commands 
select_cor_table(1,0) and
select_cor_table(2,0) 
to switch from one file to the other.
Notes 
• An RTC4 mit Option “3D” can store only one 
correction file. 
• A second correction file can only be used, if a 
RTC4D2.HEX (= DSP Program File for 2D 
user programs has been loaded.

[p.63]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
5 Advanced Programming 
63
innovators for industry
5.4
Using Multiple 
RTC4 PCIe Boards in One PC
The RTC4DLL.dll and RTC4DLLx64.dll support the 
control of up to 16 RTC4 boards in a single PC.
The RTC4 boards work completely independently of 
each other. Command lists for each board can be 
loaded and executed at any time.
If several RTC4 boards are used, the 
RTC4 command set supports two different methods 
for writing user programs: 
• Multi-Board Programming, Page 63 
• Sequential Programming, Page 63 
5.4.1
Multi-Board Programming
In this programming method, the multi-board 
versions (command names with prefix n_) of the 
RTC4 commands are used. 
Each of these multi-board commands allows 
specifying the number of the RTC4 board that shall 
receive the command. 
To use a multi-board command, proceed as follows:
(1) Add the prefix n_ to the command name.
(2) Include the number of the RTC4 board to 
which the command shall be sent as the first 
parameter (unsigned 16-bit value). The 
remaining parameters of the command – if any – 
are the same as for the normal (single board) 
command.
The installed RTC4 boards are numbered in the order 
given by the PCIe bus (from 1 to a maximum of 16). 
get_serial_number (see example (3) below) or 
get_serial_number_32 can be used to determine 
which RTC4 boards have been assigned numbers 1 to 
16. rtc4_count_cards can be used to check how 
many boards are detected by the DLL.
All multi-board commands are listed in Chapter 11, 
“RTC4 Commands”, Page 113.
Examples in Pascal 
(1) n_jump_abs(1, 500, 500)
writes a jump[*] Command to the point (500, 
500) into the current list of RTC4 board #1.
(2) n_execute_list(RTC4_no, list_no)
executes list number list_no (1 or 2) on the RTC4 
board with the number specified by the variable 
RTC4_no.
(3) sn_1 := n_get_serial_number(1)
returns the serial number of board #1.
5.4.2
Sequential Programming
For sequential programming, the command 
select_rtc activates one of the installed RTC4 boards. 
In this programming method, the normal (single 
board) commands can be used. The multi-board 
commands are not affected by select_rtc. All 
commands following the select_rtc command are 
sent to the selected board until the command 
select_rtc is used again. The specified RTC4 number 
must not be larger than the total number of RTC4 
boards. See also rtc4_count_cards.
Care must be taken if several programs or processes 
are running on one system simultaneously, because 
select_rtc immediately redirects the output of all 
currently running processes to the specified RTC4 
board.
Notice!
• select_rtc defines the active RTC4 board 
(“working board”) for all programs (threads) 
that are currently running. 
In multi-tasking or multi-threaded 
user programs, this can result in programming 
errors. For such user programs, only multi-
board commands should be used, see 
Chapter 5.4.1, “Multi-Board Programming”, 
Page 63.

[p.64]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
5 Advanced Programming 
64
innovators for industry
5.5
RTC4-Circular Queue Mode
In RTC4-Circular Queue Mode the full capacity of the 
RTC4 memory of 8000 commands is used. 
Instead of using two list memories with 4000 entries 
each (as described in Chapter 4.1, “Software 
Concept”, Page 24), the list commands can be 
transferred to the RTC4 board without any limit and 
continuously. 
While some of the stored commands are still being 
processed, other memory locations can already be 
loaded with new commands. 
Initialization
RTC4-Circular Queue Mode is enabled by 
set_list_mode(1). It should be called at the 
beginning of the user program, that is, before 
writing any list commands.
Start
After enabling the RTC4-Circular Queue Mode, users 
can immediately start writing list commands to the 
RTC4. set_start_list must not be used here. 
Execution starts automatically when the number of 
stored commands exceeds 4000 or if set_end_of_list 
is used.
While the RTC4 executes a block of entered 
commands, it is possible to load the circular queue 
with subsequent list commands. When the end of the 
list buffer is reached, the RTC4 starts over at the 
beginning. However, the RTC4 overwrites only those 
commands which have already been executed. See 
also Note (2), right.
Continuous Data Transfer
After execution of the first block of commands, 
the RTC4 checks if the next block of 4000 commands 
is ready for execution. If this is the case, the next 
block is executed immediately afterwards.
Usually it takes much less time to write a list than to 
execute it. Therefore, it should be no problem to 
write the next block of commands during execution 
of the current block.
Termination
At the end of the application, usually a block of less 
than 4000 commands remains. 
To ensure correct execution of this last block, it must 
be terminated by set_end_of_list.
Controlling Program Execution
If it is necessary, stop_execution can be called to 
immediately stop program execution in the 
RTC4-Circular Queue Mode. In addition, calling 
stop_list pauses execution and calling restart_list 
resumes execution.
Notes 
(1) When the RTC4-Circular Queue Mode is active, 
only the following list handling commands can be 
used:
– set_end_of_list 
– stop_execution 
– stop_list 
– restart_list 
Do not use any of the other list handling 
commands, see Chapter 11, “RTC4 Commands”, 
Page 113.
(2) Avoid writing a list command into a full circular 
queue because the call of that command 
automatically waits until it can enter the circular 
queue. On the one hand, this guarantees that no 
list command gets lost, but on the other hand, 
the RTC4 does not handle new control commands 
during that lock period – in contrast, control 
commands are immediately processed when the 
circular queue is not full.
4 For preventing a lock period, call get_list_space, 
which returns the remaining capacity of the 
circular queue (which has a total capacity of 8000 
commands). For relieving the RTC4, it is a good 
idea to keep track of the remaining capacity 
instead of calling get_list_space each time 
before trying to write a list command into the 
circular queue.

[p.65]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
5 Advanced Programming 
65
innovators for industry
5.6
Structured Programming
The command set of the RTC4 includes a number of 
list commands for controlling program flow:
• set_list_jump
• list_call
• list_call_cond
• list_jump_cond
• list_return
• list_nop
These commands use the RTC4 list memory as a single 
list buffer with a total capacity of 8000 entries. Each 
command in the list buffer has a unique address in 
the range [0…7999]. 
Together with the control commands 
set_input_pointer and execute_at_pointer, they 
allow structured list programming and execution.
5.6.1
Input Pointer
To write a list of commands to a particular location 
in the RTC4 list memory, set_input_pointer is used 
(instead of set_start_list_1 or set_start_list_2). 
set_input_pointer places the Input Pointer at the 
desired address in the range [0…7999]. The next 
list command is written to this address. 
The current position of the Input Pointer can be 
queried by calling get_input_pointer.
5.6.2
List Jumps
set_list_jump defines a jump to the specified 
address. During execution, the RTC4 jumps to this 
address.
Similarly, list_call defines a jump to a subroutine 
(sub-list). The sub-list has to be terminated by 
list_return. The main list must be terminated by 
set_end_of_list.
5.6.3
Conditional List Jumps
Conditional list jumps and subroutine calls can be 
programmed by list_jump_cond and list_call_cond: 
The current value of the 16-bit digital input port at 
the EXTENSION 1 socket connector is read and, 
depending on its value, a subroutine is called or a 
jump within a list is executed. See also Chapter 5.6.5, 
“Programming Examples (Pascal)”, Page 66 and 
Chapter 6.7.1, “16-Bit Digital Input and 
16-Bit Digital Output”, Page 90.
5.6.4
Output Pointer
To start execution at a particular address, the 
command execute_at_pointer must be used. The 
RTC4 starts execution immediately.
Execution stops when a set_end_of_list command is 
encountered. If the end of the list buffer is reached, 
the RTC4 continues at the address zero. 
If the external start signal is used, see Chapter 4.1.4, 
“External Control Inputs”, Page 26, 
set_extstartpos_list allows definition of the start 
address for the external list start. 
Notice!
• If the end of the list buffer is reached during 
writing of a list, the Input Pointer is reset to 
zero, that is, the next list command is written to 
the address zero. 
• Make sure not to overwrite any commands 
which are still needed by your application. 
Notice!
• If the end of the list buffer is reached during 
execution (and if the last command is not a 
set_end_of_list command), execution 
continues at the address zero.

[p.66]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
5 Advanced Programming 
66
innovators for industry
5.6.5
Programming Examples 
(Pascal)
(1) “Confirm a signal” 
set_start_list(1); 
…
set_io_cond_list(0, 0, 1);
// set Bit #0 of the 16-bit digital output port 
list_jump_cond(0, 1, get_input_pointer);
// loop until the signal is confirmed 
// (i.e. Bit #0 of the digital input turns HIGH) 
clear_io_cond_list(0, 0, 1);
// clear Bit #0 of the 16-bit output 
list_jump_cond(1, 0, get_input_pointer);
// loop until the signal is confirmed 
… 
set_end_of_list;

innovators for industry
5.7
Pixel Output Mode
Vector Commands are intended for scanning vector 
based images. However, the RTC4 also allows 
reproduction of bitmaps. That means black-
and-white images and even greyscale images can be 
created with a suitably prepared laser. Furthermore, 
pixel and vector based images can be combined as 
desired.
5.7.1
Principle of Operation
A raster image is created line by line, where each line 
consists of a number of equidistant pixels. A line is 
reproduced in a single scan. During this scan, the 
laser focus (set position) moves from one pixel to the 
next at a constant time rate. The pixel distance and 
the output period can be set by users.
Each pixel is usually marked by one laser pulse. For 
black-and-white images the laser is turned on or off 
at each pixel. Greyscale images can be created by 
modulating the laser power or the pulse length of the 
laser pulse for each individual pixel or, alternatively, 
by varying the number of laser pulses per pixel. 
Refer to the Chapter 5.7.5, “Laser Control”, Page 72 
for details.
5.7.2
Command Calls
Before starting an image line, a jump[*] Command to 
the start position of the line must be performed. 
The image line itself starts by set_pixel_line. 
set_pixel_line turns on the pixel output mode, sets 
the pixel output period T and defines the distance in 
the X and Y directions (dx, dy) between two adjacent 
pixels in the line. 
Pixel Output
Each pixel in the image line is then created by one 
set_pixel command. This command defines the 
pulse width of the LASERON signal for the pixel. 
In addition, an analog output signal (ANALOG OUT2, 
10-bit resolution) can be specified for each pixel. 
The analog signal is transmitted synchronously with 
the pixel output (scanner position) and can be used 
for modulating the laser power, see Chapter 5.7.5, 
“Laser Control”, Page 72.
Data Input (Optional)
If the RTC4 is used together with an I/O Extension 
Board for RTC3/RTC4 (Accessory), set_pixel also 
allows data to be read from an analog input port 
synchronous to pixel output. The input data is 
written to the RTC4 list buffer, from where it can later 
be read. For further details, see set_pixel and 
read_pixel_ad.

[p.68]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
5 Advanced Programming 
68
innovators for industry
Notes 
• set_pixel_line and set_pixel are list commands, 
that is, they are written into a list.
• set_pixel_line requires two list entries in the list 
buffer memory.
• The number of pixels in an image line is limited 
only by the capacity of the RTC4 list buffer, see 
Chapter 15, “Technical Specifications 
– RTC4 PCIe Board”, Page 212. It is suggested – 
especially for large bitmaps – to set up a new list 
for each image line to avoid a list change during 
the execution of one line.
• Each image line must start with a set_pixel_line 
command.
• The set_pixel calls for the individual pixels must 
follow immediately after set_pixel_line. The first 
subsequent command in the list which is not a 
set_pixel command turns off the 
Pixel Output Mode.
• The pixel distance (dx, dy) in x direction and 
y direction (in bits) is to be specified in 64-bit IEEE 
floating point format. This allows scaling and 
rotating the image without rounding errors. 
However, the actual output coordinates (in bits) 
of each individual pixel are always rounded to 
integer values.
• The pixel output period can be any multiple of 
10 µs, whereas the standard output period for 
the Microsteps in the vector mode is always 
10 µs. See also Chapter 5.7.4, 
“Galvanometer Scanner Control”, Page 70.

RTC4 Manual

innovators for industry
5.7.3
Synchronization
Figure 20 shows the pixel output timing diagram. 
Each LASERON pulse is preceded by a LaserOn Delay. 
The ANALOG OUT2 signal is synchronous to the 
position update of the scanners. Also observe the 
information given in Chapter 5.7.5, “Laser Control”, 
Page 72.
Notes 
• The digital-to-analog converter requires about 
1 µs…2 µs to produce a stable analog output 
signal.
The pixel output period T is set by set_pixel_line. To 
avoid laser control faults, make sure that the 
following holds true for all pixels:
T LaserOn Delay + 
maximum pulse length + 10 µs
Notes 
• The 9-pin D-SUB laser connector of the RTC4 
provides the signals LASER1 and LASERON or 
ANALOG OUT2. If the ANALOG OUT2 signal is to 
be used, jumper X7 must be set to Position 2-3. 
See also Chapter 9.1, “Checking Jumper 
Settings”, Page 95.
• If you need the LASERON signal and the 
ANALOG OUT2 signal, then you have the 
possibility of setting jumper X7 to Position 1-2 
and taking the signal ANALOG OUT2 from pin 14 
of the MARKING ON THE FLY connector.
Laser Parameters 
• The LaserOn Delay should be set to a suitable 
value, see also Chapter 5.7.4, 
“Galvanometer Scanner Control”, Page 70.
• The standby pulses should be turned off in pixel 
mode, see set_standby.
• Usually each pixel is marked with one pulse. To 
accomplish this, the pulse length and output 
period of the LASER1 signal / LASER2 signal 
should be set to values larger than the pixel 
output period T, see set_laser_timing. 
Thus the LASER1 signal / LASER2 signal stays 
active and the laser is controlled by the 
LASERON signal only. See also Chapter 5.7.5, 
“Laser Control”, Page 72.
• The FirstPulseKiller signal should be set to zero 
(YAG modes only).

5.7.4
Galvanometer Scanner Control
The RTC4 supports two different modes for scanning 
raster images: 
• Mode 0, Page 70 
• Mode 1, Page 71 
The two modes differ in the way the 
galvanometer scanners are controlled when moving 
from one pixel position to the next. The scanner 
mode is set by set_pixel_line. 
Mode 0
In Mode 0, the scanner “jumps“ from one pixel to the 
next in one step. Depending on the pixel distance, 
some overshooting may occur, see Figure 21.
• In Mode 0, the pixel output period T must be 
relatively long: T 1 ms…10 ms.
• To make sure that each pixel is marked at its exact 
position, the LaserOn Delay must be longer than 
the required settling time. 
In Mode 0, each pixel is marked at its exact position. 
The position does not depend on the dynamic 
performance of the scan head. However, 
the overall marking time is quite long. 
Mode 0 should always be used if one pixel is marked 
by more than one laser pulse.
21
Timing of laser and scanner control in Pixel Output Mode 
Mode 0. 
Scanner Position
LASERON Signal
LaserOn
Delay
Time
Time
Real Position
Set Position
Laser pulse should
not start before
the set position is
reached.
Mode 0

[p.71]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
5 Advanced Programming 
71
innovators for industry
Mode 1
In Mode 1, each image line is treated like a normal 
vector. That means the motion is split up into a 
number of Microsteps. After an initial acceleration 
phase, the laser focus moves with an approximately 
constant velocity along the entire image line. The 
pixels are marked in passing.
• In Mode 1, the LaserOn Delay can be set to a few 
microseconds.
• The pixel period T can be chosen according to the 
suitable laser frequency. It is typically about 
50 µs.
• The entire image is slightly shifted because of the 
Tracking Error. This can be compensated by 
adjusting the start position of each image line.
• To suppress distortions during the acceleration 
phase, some idle pixels (with zero pulse length) 
can be inserted at the beginning of each image 
line.

Mode 1. The pixel output period in this example is T = 
50 µs (= 5 Microsteps).

innovators for industry
5.7.5
Laser Control
Black-and-White Images
For black-and-white images, the pulse length 
is set either to zero (“black“ pixel) or to a 
suitable constant value (“white“ pixel).
To enhance the contrast, several pulses (instead of 
only one) per pixel can be set. To do this, the pulse 
length of the LASER1 signal (Q-Switch) has to be set 
accordingly, see set_laser_timing.
Notes 
• If several pulses are used for one pixel, Mode 0 
should be used, see Chapter 5.7.4, 
“Galvanometer Scanner Control”, Page 70.
Greyscale Images
The laser energy discharged at each pixel position can 
be varied in different ways:
• (A) To Vary the Laser Pulse Length, Page 72 
• (B) To Vary the Laser Power, Page 72 
• (C) To Vary the Number of Laser Pulses per Pixel, 
Page 72 
The most suitable method depends greatly on the 
type of laser employed.
(A) To Vary the Laser Pulse Length
Some laser types allow varying the pulse length 
(duration) of each laser pulse. set_pixel defines the 
pulse length of the LASERON signal for each pixel in 
units of 1/8 µs (The pulse length of the 
LASERON signal is equal to the pulse length of the 
laser pulse, if pulse length and output period of the 
LASER1 signal / LASER2 signal are larger then the 
pixel output period T).
Notes 
• It is recommended that some experiments be 
performed to determine the appropriate pulse 
length range for producing a smooth greyscale. 
The resulting pixel “colors“ (greyscale values) 
strongly depend on the employed material and 
on the laser.
(B) To Vary the Laser Power
Q-switched lasers in particular produce very short 
laser pulses which cannot be easily varied in length. 
For such lasers, it is more suitable to create greyscale 
values by modulating the laser pulse energy. One 
possibility is to vary the laser power with a suitable 
modulating device (for example, an AOM).
set_pixel allows specification of a 10-bit output 
value for each pixel. The value is transferred to the 
ANALOG OUT2 port of the RTC4 synchronously to the 
pixel output, see also Chapter 5.7.3, 
“Synchronization”, Page 69.
The ANALOG OUT2 port is optionally available 
at the 9-pin D-SUB Laser Connector. The output 
range is 0 V…10 V, see Chapter 9.1.2, “Signals at the 
Laser Connector”, Page 96.
(C) To Vary the Number of Laser Pulses per Pixel
Another way to modulate the laser pulse energy of 
Q-switched lasers with short laser pulses is to vary the 
number of laser pulses per pixel. For this purpose one 
can vary either the length (pulse length) of the 
LASERON signal, which serves as a gate for the 
individual laser pulses, see Figure 20, or (with 
set_laser_timing) the pulse length and the output 
period of the individual laser pulses.
Notes 
• For this method, Mode 0 should be used, see 
Chapter 5.7.4, “Galvanometer Scanner Control”, 
Page 70.

[p.73]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
5 Advanced Programming 
73
innovators for industry
5.8
Timed Jump Commands, 
Timed Mark Commands
Vector Commands (jump[*] Commands and 
mark[*] Commands) are processed by the RTC4 in 
such a way that the laser focus moves along the 
surface of the Image Field with a defined speed, the 
jump_speed or the mark_speed. This is fine for most 
laser marking and laser material processing 
applications.
However, some applications require that each jump 
vector or mark vector consumes exactly the same 
amount of time, regardless of its length. In this case, 
it is practical to specify the duration of the jump, 
rather than the jump speed.
The timed commands 
• timed_jump_abs 
• timed_jump_rel 
• timed_mark_abs 
• timed_mark_rel 
allow specification of the duration with an accuracy 
of 10 µs (the output period of the Microsteps) and in 
the range from 10 µs to 655350 µs ( 0.6 s).
The jump (mark) vector is split up into a number of 
Microsteps that correspond exactly to the specified 
time, see Chapter 4.2.3, “Microstepping”, Page 29. 
Of course, this means that the jump (mark) speed, 
that is, the velocity of the laser focus (and the 
angular velocity of the motion of the mirrors) depend 
on the length of the vector.
Notes 
• After a timed jump (mark) command, a 
Jump Delay, a Mark Delay or a Polygon Delay 
is inserted, just like after a normal 
jump[*] Command or mark[*] Command. That 
means the total time for the command is the sum 
of the specified time and the corresponding 
delay. See also Chapter 4.3.2, “Scanner Delays”, 
Page 33.
• Durations for jump[*] Commands or 
mark[*] Commands can be specified with 2D 
vectors only.

[p.74]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
5 Advanced Programming 
74
innovators for industry
5.9
Automatic Self-Calibration
Purpose of Use
Environmental fluctuations, especially temperature 
changes, can cause galvanometer scanner drift, that 
is, a shift (offset drift) and an increase or decrease in 
size (gain drift) of the Image Field. 
Therefore, high-precision applications should only be 
started up when the scanners have reached their 
operating temperature. In addition, the magnitudes 
of environmental fluctuations, for example, 
operating temperature changes, to which the 
scan head is exposed should be kept as small as 
possible. 
For applications that are subjected to unavoidable 
environmental fluctuations, but nevertheless require 
high long-term repeatability, SCANLAB provides 
scan heads with automatic self-calibration (optional 
for apertures 14 mm). Scan heads delivered with 
this option have an internal reference sensor system. 
This reference system provides the RTC4 software 
with the ability to automatically calibrate the 
galvanometer scanner position detectors any time 
users deem it appropriate. Thus, drift effects can be 
reliably compensated and positioning accuracy is 
maintained over long periods of time. Remaining 
long-term drift effects are the same order of 
magnitude as short-term repeatability.
Operating Principle
Automatic self calibration of the scan head is started 
by auto_cal(…,1). 
During calibration, the scan head automatically seeks 
several reference positions within the scannable area 
that are defined by the internal sensor system. The 
seek values are determined and compared with fixed 
reference values. From the resulting deviations, offset 
and gain compensation factors are calculated. 
These compensation factors are immediately made 
available for use in all future positioning tasks until 
auto_cal(…,1) is called again or until compensation is 
shut off by auto_cal(…,2). The entire calibration 
procedure takes place in about 5 seconds.
auto_cal(…,0) is available for determining the 
reference values. The resulting output coordinates for 
the various sensor positions are stored in non-volatile 
memory on the RTC4 board. This way, the values are 
not lost when the system is shut down.
The laser must be switched off during the calibration 
procedure and no other commands are transferred to 
the scan head until the calibration is completed.
For further information, see auto_cal.

[p.75]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
5 Advanced Programming 
75
innovators for industry
5.10 3D Functionality
• 3D Commands are part of the standard software 
package for RTC4 PCIe Boards and RTC4 Ethernet 
Boards. 
• 3D Functionality can only be used together with 
RTC4 PCIe Boards and RTC4 Ethernet Boards with 
enabled Option “3D”. 
5.10.1 Intended Use
The 3D Functionality is intended for controlling a 
3-axis scan system consisting of an xy scan head and 
a z axis dynamic focusing unit (varioSCAN) controlled 
by an 
• RTC4 PCIe Board and RTC4 Ethernet Board 
with enabled Option “3D” 
3D user programs can be realized using a 3-axis 
scan system with or without an F-Theta objective. 
The 3D Functionality can be used in either case. 
However, note each scan system needs its individual 
correction file. 
5.10.2 Principle of Operation
A location within the processing space shall be 
designated by the point (x|y|z). To move the laser 
focus to this point, the 3-axis scan system must 
perform two tasks:
(1) The mirrors in the xy scan head must deflect the 
laser beam in the correct direction.
(2) The focal length of the z axis unit must be 
adjusted so that the center of the laser focus 
moves to the point (x|y|z).
For controlling the varioSCAN, the RTC control board 
board first calculates the focal length shift between 
the points (x|y|z) and (0|0|0). This focal length shift 
l is then transformed into an output value for the 
varioSCAN by a lookup table. The lookup table is 
included in the 3D correction file. 
z Axis Calibration
The z axis output table is calculated by SCANLAB 
using all available optical data of the individual 3-axis 
system. 
However, z axis calibration depends strongly on 
parameters such as divergence of the input laser 
beam and tolerances of the optical elements – 
information which is not available to SCANLAB. 
Therefore, in some cases it can be necessary to re-
calibrate the z axis output table in situ. 
See also Chapter 5.10.7 “Calibrating z Axes”, 
Page 78. 
Specifying Coordinate Values
In the RTC command set, the desired x values, 
y values and z values must be specified as bit values, 
which are calculated by multiplying the desired 
values in [mm] with the calibration factor K in 
[bits/mm]. 
In varioSCAN manual, technical specifications 
chapter, you can find: 
• Calibration factor K in [bits/mm]
• Usable Image Field size in [mm2] 
• Allowed range for the z values in [mm] 
(maximum focus shift in z direction)

[p.76]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
5 Advanced Programming 
76
innovators for industry
5.10.3 Installation
The RTC4 DLL from the standard software package 
already includes all 2D commands and 
3D Commands. It can be used both for 2D 
user programs and 3D user programs. 
• Installation of the RTC4 software is described in: 
– Chapter 10, “Software for the 
RTC4 PCIe Board”, Page 101 
– Chapter 16.3, “Installation and Operation”, 
Page 228 
• If you want to use a RTC4 board for 3D 
user programs, then a different DSP Program File 
(= RTC4D3.HEX) must be used, see Chapter 5.10.5 
“Initializing the User Program”, Page 76. 
• Furthermore, you need a 3D correction file. 
• The 3D files can only be loaded if the Option “3D” 
is enabled on your RTC4 board. 
To check this, call get_rtc_version. 
5.10.4 Operating a varioSCAN at 
“2.SCANHEAD” Connector – 
Prerequisites
In order to operate a varioSCAN at the 
“2.SCANHEAD” connector of an RTC4 board it is not 
sufficient to simply connect it.
In addition, make sure that the following 
prerequisites are fulfilled: 
(1) On the RTC4 board, the Option “Second 
Scan Head Control” must be enabled. 
(2) Your user program must enable the second 
scan head, that is, 
select_cor_table(1, 1) 
must be in the source code. 
5.10.5 Initializing the User Program 
The initialization sequence that should begin a 
3D user program is basically the same as for a 
2D user program. 
• However, make sure to load the correct 
DSP Program File = RTC4D3.HEX.

[p.77]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
5 Advanced Programming 
77
innovators for industry
5.10.6 3D Commands
Notes 
• See also 3D-Control Commands, Page 114. 
• See also 3D-List Commands, Page 115. 
3D Vector Commands
3D Vector Commands are:
• jump_abs_3d 
• jump_rel_3d 
• mark_abs_3d 
• mark_rel_3d 
3D Vector Commands allow moving to any point 
(x|y|z) in the 3D processing space. The syntax is the 
same as for the corresponding Notes on 2D Vector 
Commands, except for the additional parameter z. 
The output period for 3D vectors is the same as for 
2D vectors (usually 10 µs). 
Other 3D Commands
Defocusing
set_defocus and set_defocus_list can be used for 
defocusing the laser spot on the target surface.
Controlling Scan System Directly 
goto_xyz and get_xyz_pos are available for direct 
scan system control. They are similar to the 2D 
commands goto_xy and get_xy_pos.
Calibrating the z Axis
load_z_table and get_z_distance are only needed 
for re-calibrating the z axis, see Chapter 5.10.7 
“Calibrating z Axes”, Page 78. 
load_z_table defines a new correction table for the 
z axis output values. load_z_table should be called 
after loading a 3D correction file.
get_z_distance returns the relative focus shift l of the 
z axis (in bits) between the point (x|y|z) and the 
point (0|0|0).
Teach-in
It can be helpful to determine the 3D coordinates of 
points that need to be laser-processed 
experimentally. This can be realized as a teach-in 
process: first the pointer laser is directed to a 
particular point by searching for suitable xy 
coordinates (xin, yin) in a user-defined plane (z = zin). 
Then the difference LL0 between the focus length of 
point (xin, yin, zin) and the focus length of point (0, 0, 
zin) is determined by a distance measurement. From 
this focus length difference you can calculate the 
desired coordinates (x, y, z) of the measured point 
with the help of teachin. 
Notes on 2D Vector Commands
• Many user programs only use a flat processing 
field (z = 0), with the varioSCAN being used for 
adjusting the focal length. In this case, the Notes 
on 2D Vector Commands (jump_abs, jump_rel, 
mark_abs and mark_rel) can be used instead of 
the 3D Vector Commands. The z coordinate of 
the vector end point will be set to zero. When 
moving from one point (x1|y1|0) to another 
point (x2|y2|0), the focal length of the varioSCAN 
is continuously adjusted so that the laser focus 
always stays in the plane z = 0. 
• During a marking process, it is important to 
maintain a sharp focus at all times. Therefore, the 
focal length is readjusted accordingly while a 
marking command is being processed.
In contrast, during execution of a jump command 
readjustment of the focus is not really necessary. 
Therefore, set_delay_mode allows defining the 
value of its directmove3d parameter to provide a 
jumping method that changes the focal length 
directly (linearly) from its start value to its end 
value. This can help to avoid needless motions of 
the z scan system (varioSCAN).

[p.78]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
5 Advanced Programming 
78
innovators for industry
5.10.7 Calibrating z Axes
The z output value Z is a function of the focus shift 
value l. This function can be approximated by a 
parabolic curve (Z = A + Bl +Cl2). The aim of the 
calibration procedure is to determine suitable 
coefficient values A, B and C. These are subsequently 
transmitted to the RTC-control board by 
load_z_table. 
The procedure for determining the coefficients A, B 
and C is described in: 
• “Calibrating a 3-Axis Laser Scan System” Manual 
(contact your SCANLAB sales engineer) 
Test for 3D Scan Systems with F-Theta 
Objectives
• With the new correction table, draw two identical 
large squares using the 3D Vector Commands. 
Draw one of the squares with the work piece in z-
position z = zmin, the other one with z = zmax. 
• These two squares should match exactly. If this is 
not the case, your correction file is not perfectly 
suited to your objective. To solve this problem, 
measure the size (x and y) of both squares and 
report these values to SCANLAB. You will then 
receive a new correction file.

[p.79]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
5 Advanced Programming 
79
innovators for industry
5.11 Processing-on-the-fly 
Functionality
• Processing-on-the-fly Commands are part of the 
standard software package for RTC4 PCIe Boards 
and RTC4 Ethernet Boards. 
5.11.1 Intended Use
The Option Processing-on-the-fly for RTC4 boards 
enables processing of parts in motion. For example, 
parts on a conveyor belt or on a rotating plate. 
The vectors transferred to the scan head are modified 
by the RTC-control board in accordance with the 
motion of the parts to be processed. 
Focusing a laser beam on a flat surface is possible if 
the scan head is equipped with an F-Theta lens. 
The Option Processing-on-the-fly can also be used in 
combination with a SCANLAB varioSCAN dynamic 
focusing unit. 
5.11.2 Principle of Operation
• Detecting the Motion of the Object, Page 79 
• Compensating Motion During Marking, Page 80 
• Synchronizing Start of Markings, Page 82 
• Monitoring Processing-on-the-fly Overflows, 
Page 82 
Detecting the Motion of the Object
For taking account of the varying speed of moving 
parts, the RTC-control board can evaluate the signals 
of a user-supplied incremental encoder, which might 
be coupled to a conveyor belt or to a rotating plate. 
The encoder signals are fed into a 32-bit counter on 
the RTC-control board. 
Each encoder input port is designed for a pair of 
standardized differential input signals (RS-422), see 
MARKING ON THE FLY Connector and Figure 30. 
Figure 23 shows the timing diagram of typical 
encoder signals. Encoder signal 2 is usually phase-
shifted by 90°. The RTC counter triggers at each edge 
of both signals, that is, one encoder increment results 
in 4 RTC counter pulses (counts). 
23
Timing diagram: typical encoder signals and corresponding 
RTC counter pulses. 
Encoder rotation angle
RTC 
counter
pulses
Encoder
signal 2
Encoder
signal 1
Encoder increment
(360°)

[p.80]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
5 Advanced Programming 
80
innovators for industry
The 90° phase-shift of the encoder signal 2 allows the 
RTC-control board to detect the speed as well as the 
direction of the motion. Depending on the direction 
of motion, the counter value (signed 32-bit) is 
increased or decreased. 
For linear motions of the parts to be processed the 
RTC-control board provides 2 separate encoder 
input ports, see Figure 30: 
• ENCODER X 
• ENCODER Y 
Thus, two independent perpendicular motions can be 
detected.
For rotational motions only 1 encoder input port is 
necessary. Here, ENCODER X must be used. 
For the input signals of the user-supplied encoder, the 
allowed Frequency is 2 MHz. 
Simulating an Encoder
If the object moves at a constant speed, an encoder 
is often not necessary. A periodic clock signal can be 
used instead. 
The RTC-control board provides a 1 MHz clock signal 
for this purpose, see simulate_encoder. 
Compensating Motion During Marking
While the object is moving, all vector data transferred 
to the scan head must be corrected, depending on 
the object’s current position. The correction is started 
by list commands. The corresponding calibration 
parameters have to be predetermined. 
In this Chapter: 
• Compensating Linear Motions, Page 80 
• Compensating Rotary Motions, Page 81 
Compensating Linear Motions
The correction for a linear motion is started by the 
list commands set_fly_x or set_fly_y. 
A Scaling Factor [in bits per count] must thereby be 
specified, which defines the shift [in bits] of the 
current output position in the Image Field(1) 
corresponding to one counter pulse. 
When set_fly_x (set_fly_y) is called, the counter is set 
to zero. Thereafter, the product of the Scaling Factor 
and the current counter value is added to the current 
output position. 
This correction is performed every 10 µs.
The Processing-on-the-fly correction can be stopped 
(simultaneously for both directions) by fly_return. 
The new output position can be thereby defined. 
(1) For the Image Field coordinate system, see Chapter 4.4, 
“Image Field Size”, Page 45.

[p.81]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
5 Advanced Programming 
81
innovators for industry
Scaling Factor 
If an encoder is used to detect the 
Processing-on-the-fly speed of the parts to be 
processed, the encoder must be calibrated before the 
Processing-on-the-fly software compensation can be 
used.
In a first step, get_encoder must be used to 
determine the encoder increment ix and iy [in counts 
per mm] for each direction: 
• Read the counter start value by get_encoder 
• Begin the motion
• Stop the motion 
• Read the counter end value by get_encoder 
• Measure the distance travelled in mm 
• Encoder increment i = (counter end value – 
counter start value) / distance travelled 
In a second step calculate the Scaling Factors kx and 
ky [in bits per count] from the encoder increments 
and the scan system calibration factor K [in bits per 
mm; specified in its manual]: 
• kx = K / ix 
• ky = K / iy 
If the object moves at a constant speed vx or vy [in 
mm per s] and the encoder simulation (see Chapter , 
“Simulating an Encoder”, Page 80) is used instead of 
an encoder, then the Scaling Factor kx and ky are 
calculated as follows: 
• kx = K × vx / (1,000,000 counts/second) 
• ky = K × vy / (1,000,000 counts/second) 
It might be necessary to correct the implied direction 
of motion by inverting the sign of kx and ky.
Compensating Rotary Motions
Before starting the correction for a rotary motion, the 
rotation center of the Processing-on-the-fly rotation 
must be defined. Therefore, the list command 
set_rot_center is provided. The rotation center may 
also be situated outside the Image Field. 
The correction itself is started by the list command 
set_fly_rot. The encoder resolution [in counts per 
revolution] must thereby be specified. The inverse of 
the encoder resolution defines the rotation [in 
number of revolutions] of the current output position 
in the Image Field corresponding to one counter 
pulse (see below). 
When set_fly_rot is called, the counter is set to zero. 
The output value is then calculated from the current 
output position, the encoder resolution and the 
current counter value. This correction is performed 
every 10 µs. 
To stop Processing-on-the-fly correction, fly_return 
should be used. The new output position can be 
thereby defined. 
Encoder Resolution 
If an encoder is used to detect the rotating speed of 
the parts to be processed, the encoder must be 
calibrated before the Processing-on-the-fly software 
compensation can be used. 
get_encoder must be used to determine the encoder 
resolution [in counts per revolution]: 
Read the counter start value by get_encoder 
Begin the rotation (a certain number of full rotations) 
Count the number of revolutions 
Stop the rotation 
Read the counter end value by get_encoder 
resolution = (counter end value – counter start 
value) / number of revolutions

[p.82]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
5 Advanced Programming 
82
innovators for industry
Note that the number of counts per revolution is 
4 times the number of encoder pulses per revolution, 
see Chapter , “Detecting the Motion of the Object”, 
Page 79. 
If the object rotates at a constant speed  [in number 
of revolutions per s] and the encoder simulation (see 
Chapter , “Simulating an Encoder”, Page 80) is used 
instead of an encoder, then the encoder resolution is 
calculated as follows: 
• resolution = (1,000,000 counts / s) /  
It might be necessary to correct the implied direction 
of motion by inverting the sign of 
encoder resolution. 
Synchronizing Start of Markings
The transfer of marking commands from the RTC list 
memory to the scan head can be started by: 
• an RTC command 
• an external /START signal 
The MARKING ON THE FLY Connector on a 
RTC-control board provides START and STOP inputs, 
see Figure 30. 
It is possible, for example, to connect the external 
start signal to a light barrier or a mechanical contact.
A delay (that is, a number of encoder pulses) between 
the START pulse and the actual start of the marking 
routine can be programmed with 
set_ext_start_delay or set_ext_start_delay_list. 
To execute several lists with defined spacings, the 
list command simulate_ext_start can be used. 
Monitoring Processing-on-the-fly Overflows
For monitoring Processing-on-the-fly overflows, 
get_marking_info is available. 
Processing-on-the-fly overflows can occur, if: 
• A Processing-on-the-fly user program is not 
optimized for the intended Processing-on-the-fly 
speed 
• An accidental considerable increase of the 
Processing-on-the-fly speed occurs during 
operation. In this case, the 
galvanometer scanners of the scan system reach 
their extreme positions and intended pattern is 
not marked 
Regularly calling get_marking_info during test runs 
or during normal operation helps to avoid failures 
caused by Processing-on-the-fly overflows.

[p.83]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US

innovators for industry
5.11.3 Installation
For Processing-on-the-fly user programs, no 
additional software is needed. The RTC4 DLL from the 
standard software package already includes all 
necessary RTC commands. 
To be able to use the Processing-on-the-fly 
Commands, the Option Processing-on-the-fly must 
be enabled on your RTC-control board. 
For checking, use get_rtc_version.

6
Electrical Connections
6.1
Laser Connector
Figure 24 shows the pin out of the 9-pin D-SUB 
connector for the laser. 
The signals at pin (2) and pin (4) are set by 
corresponding jumpers, see Chapter 9.1.2, “Signals 
at the Laser Connector”, Page 96. 
6.1.1
Analog Output Ports
The RTC4 provides two general purpose analog 
output ports, ANALOG OUT1 and ANALOG OUT2. 
The output resolution of both ports is 10 bits.
They can be loaded either directly or by write_da_x / 
write_da_x_list via a list. 
The output voltage range of the ANALOG OUT1 port 
can be set to either 0 V…2.50 V or 0 V…10 V. 
The range of the ANALOG OUT2 port is fixed at 
0 V…10 V.
Both signals are referenced to PC ground (GND). The 
maximum current load of both signals is 5 mA.
If jumper X6 has been configured to replace the 
ANALOG OUT1 signal with +5 V, then the maximum 
current load is 100 mA at pin (4)(1) of the laser 
connector.
6.1.2
Laser Control Signals
The laser control signals LASER1 and LASER2 at 
pin (1) and pin (3)(2) differ depending on the selected 
laser mode, see the corresponding timing diagrams 
in Chapter 4.6, “Laser Control”, Page 48: 
All laser control signals (LASERON, LASER1 and 
LASER2) are digital TTL level signals (alternatively 
active-high or active-low, see Chapter 9.1.1, “TTL 
Laser Signal Level”, Page 96). They are referenced to 
GND2. The maximum current load is 10 mA.
On RTC4 Boards with Option “DC/DC Converter”, 
GND and GND2 are galvanically decoupled. 
Otherwise GND and GND2 are identical – observe the 
following safety notice.

Caution!
• There is no potential separation on all RTC4 
boards (PCI, PCIe, Ethernet, and the 
RTC SCANalone) without Option “DC/DC 
Converter”(a), that is, GND and GND2 are 
identical. Due to the direct connection between 
laser and RTC4 board supply voltages, high 
compensation currents on the signal lines may 
occur. This can result in damage of the RTC4 
board line drivers as well as of the laser inputs. 
Make sure that both the supply voltages for the 
laser and the RTC4 board are on the same 
reference potential. 
(a) Applies to RTC5 boards as well.

6.1.3
External Control Signals
The external control signals /START and /STOP 
(TTL active-low) are referenced to GND. 
Both input signals are connected internally to +5 V 
by pull-up resistors, see also Chapter 4.1.4, “External 
Control Inputs”, Page 26.

innovators for industry
6.2
LASER EXTENSION Socket 
Connector
The 26-pin socket connector LASER EXTENSION on 
the RTC4 board (on the RTC5, the corresponding 
socket connector is labeled EXTENSION 2) provides a 
buffered 8-bit digital output port (L0 to L7). 
The signals at pin (15) and pin (17) are set by 
corresponding jumpers, see Chapter 9.1.3, “Digital 
Output Port (Laser Extension Socket Connector)”, 
Page 98. 
6.2.1
8-Bit Digital Output Port
The buffered 8-bit digital output port (TTL level) is 
intended for YAG lasers with a digital lamp current 
control. However, it can be used for any other 
purpose as well.
The commands write_8bit_port and 
write_8bit_port_list load the digital output port 
with an 8-bit value. The output is in high-impedance 
mode (tri-state) until an initial value is assigned to it.
The most significant bit (MSB) (L7) of the output 
value can be used for other purposes, for example, 
for controlling a shutter. To do this, the MSB can be 
assigned to an extra pin on the LASER EXTENSION 
socket connector, see Chapter 9.1.3, “Digital Output 
Port (Laser Extension Socket Connector)”, Page 98.
6.2.2
Laser Signals
Like the laser signals of the laser connector, the 
output signals LASER1 and LASER2 depend on the 
selected laser mode, see Chapter 6.1.2, “Laser 
Control Signals”, Page 84.

6.3
SCANHEAD Connector
(connector for the first scan head) 
The 25-pin D-SUB connector for the scan head is 
compatible with most scan heads which use the 
XY2-100 standard. The pin out is shown in Figure 26. 
6.3.1
Control Signals
Data channels CHAN1 through CHAN3 transmit 
control values to the scan head. The SYNC and CLOCK 
channels transmit synchronization and clock signals 
to the scan system. 
The CHAN3 channel is optionally provided for 
controlling a third axis in a 3-axis system. 
See also Chapter 7, “Options”, Page 93. Contact 
SCANLAB for further information.
6.3.2
Status Signals
The STATUS channel receives XY2-100 standard 
compliant status signals returned by the scan system. 
Consult your scan system operating manual to 
determine which status signals are generated by your 
scan system and how they can be applied for 
monitoring purposes.
6.4
2. SCANHEAD Socket 
Connector
(connector for the second scan head) 
The 26-pin 2. SCANHEAD Socket Connector is 
designed for connecting a second scan head in a 
double scan head configuration. 
The pin out is shown in Figure 27. 
The signals on the 2. SCANHEAD Socket Connector 
are optional and must be enabled by SCANLAB, see 
also Chapter 7, “Options”, Page 93. Contact 
SCANLAB for further information. 
SCANLAB recommends using an additional slot cover 
for connecting the second scan head. A suitable slot 
cover with a 25-pin D-SUB connector – with a pin out 
as shown in Figure 26 – is available from SCANLAB.

6.5
Data Cable
To connect the scan head to the RTC4, a data cable is 
required. This cable is usually not included in the 
scope of delivery. 
SCANLAB recommends the following design:
• The data cable to connect a system without 
iDRIVE must have identical 25-pin (male) D-SUB 
connectors at both ends. The cable consists of six 
twisted cable pairs for the following six channels: 
SYNC±, CHAN1±, CHAN2±, CHAN3±, STATUS± 
and CLOCK±. The channel CHAN3± is provided 
for optionally controlling a third axis.
• The data cable must have coaxial copper braided 
shielding.
• The data cable should not be longer than 20 m. 
From a length of 12 m the synchronization of the 
bidirectional communication between scan head 
and RTC4 must be adapted, see also command 
set_piso_control. 
• The D-SUB connectors must have fully shielded 
metal housings.
• The electrical connection of the cable’s braided 
shielding to the D-SUB housing should not be 
implemented as a wire. Instead, the cable’s 
braided shielding should be coaxially connected 
to the D-SUB housing by shielded clamps.
• Applies to the RTC4 PCIe Board: The data cable’s 
scan head end must be fitted with a ferrit ring 
(for example, Würth WE 742 711 32).
• Applies to RTC4 PCIe Board and RTC4 Ethernet 
Board: The data cable’s controller end must be 
fitted with a ferrit ring (for example, 
Würth WE 742 711 31).
Figure 28 shows the data cable layout and pin 
assignments (for example, #0100686) to connect a 
system without iDRIVE. 
Some scan heads have only a single connector to 
provide both the operating voltage and the data 
signals. For these scan heads, SCANLAB recommends 
implementing a cabling solution which uses a 
separate cable for data. The cable should have the 
properties as already described, see above.

6.6
Optical Data Interface 
(Optional)
RTC4 PCIe Boards with Option “Optical Data 
Interface” are equipped with an optical data interface 
and ST ports for data transfer by a duplex optical fiber 
cable, see Figure 2. 
This allows scan systems to be controlled by optically 
transferred signals. Optical data transfer provides the 
advantages of immunity to electrical interference 
(ESD and EMC) and complete galvanic separation.
The data interface converts electrical signals into 
optical signals with a wavelength of 650 nm. The 
optical signals are guided along a polymer optical 
fiber cable to an optical data interface (optionally) 
installed in the scan system, where they are 
converted back into electrical signals. Compliant with 
the XY2-100-O protocol, the optical signals are 
transferred bidirectionally every 10 µs over 3 data 
channels.
The optical fiber cable is not included in the scope of 
delivery. 
SCANLAB recommends use of a 1 mm diameter 
duplex polymer optical fiber cable with a maximum 
length of 30 m. 
The optical fiber cable must be fitted with two ST 
plugs at each end. Use the optical fiber cable to cross-
connect (not 1:1) both ST ports (see Figure 2) of the 
RTC4 PCIe Board first scan head connector with both 
ST ports of the scan system.
If the signals for a second scan head are enabled on 
the RTC4 PCIe Board (Option “Second Scan Head 
Control”), also a second scan system can be 
connected by a polymer optical fiber.

[p.90]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US

innovators for industry
6.7
EXTENSION 1 Socket 
Connector
The EXTENSION 1 socket connector provides a 16-bit 
digital TTL input and a buffered 16-bit digital TTL 
output. 
The pin-out is shown in Figure 29. 
6.7.1
16-Bit Digital Input and 
16-Bit Digital Output
The 16-bit digital output port can be used, for 
example, for controlling a workpiece transport 
system. Signals of the transport system, of workpiece 
recognition systems etc. can be read by the 16-bit 
input port. A group of related commands facilitates 
straight-forward incorporation of port-based 
operations into user programs:
The write_io_port_list and write_io_port 
commands specify the digital output values. The 
16-bit digital output is in high-impedance mode 
(tri-state) until an initial value is assigned to it.
The read_io_port or get_io_status commands read 
the current values of the digital input or output ports.
The set_io_cond_list and clear_io_cond_list 
commands allow digital output values to be 
influenced by digital input values: Depending on the 
current value of the digital input, individual bits of 
the output port are set or cleared.
list_jump_cond and list_call_cond can be used to 
call subroutines or jump within a list – depending on 
the current value of the digital input, see also 
Chapter 5.6, “Structured Programming”, Page 65. 
6.7.2
BUSY Status
The BUSY status signal is outputted at pin (36). 
When an user program is currently executed the 
BUSY status signal is HIGH.

6.8
MARKING ON THE FLY 
Connector
Figure 30 shows the pin-out of the 
MARKING ON THE FLY Connector. For its exact 
location on the RTC-control board, see Figure 1. 
See Chapter 5.11, “Processing-on-the-fly 
Functionality”, Page 79. 
6.8.1
Encoder Input Ports
Each of the encoder input ports ENCODER X and 
ENCODER Y are designed for a pair of standardized 
RS-422 differential signals, see Figure 23. 
• HIGH level input voltage: 2.5 V 
• LOW level input voltage: 0.5 V 
• Frequency: 2 MHz 
6.8.2
DC Voltage Output
At pin (01), as a power supply for the encoders, it is 
outputted: 
• (+5 ± 0.25) V DC, referenced to GND 
• Maximum current load: 100 mA 
6.8.3
Input Ports for External 
Signals
The signal input ports /START2 and /STOP2 (TTL 
active-low) correspond to the /START and /STOP 
inputs at the 9-pin Signals at the Laser Connector, 
Page 96. 
To start list execution, either the /START signal or the 
/START2 signal can be used. 
List execution is aborted, if either the /STOP signal or 
the /STOP2 signal becomes LOW. 
START inputs must be enabled by set_control_mode. 
6.8.4
ANALOG OUT2 Output Port 
The 10-bit ANALOG OUT2 Output Port can be used 
for any purpose. To load that output port with a 
desired value, write_da_2 or write_da_2_list is 
available. 
• Voltage Range: 0 V...+10 V 
• Maximum current load: 5 mA 
6.8.5
BUSY OUT Status Signal
The BUSY OUT Status Signal is HIGH when a list is 
currently executed, see get_status. 
The maximum current load is 10 mA. 
• Voltage Range: TTL level 
• Maximum current load: 10 mA

7 Options

innovators for industry
7
Options
RTC4 PCIe Boards are available with different options 
(features). For new boards, the options ordered are 
enabled/installed at the SCANLAB factory on delivery. 
For information on retrofitting already delivered 
boards, see Section ”Notes”, Page 93. 
• Option “Second Scan Head Control” 
– Controlling Two Scan Systems 
Simultaneously 
Allows that an RTC4 PCIe Board can 
simultaneously control two xy-scan systems via 
its two scan head connectors, see also 
Chapter 5.3.1, “Double Scan Head 
Configuration”, Page 62. 
• Option Processing-on-the-fly 
Allows that a Processing-on-the-fly correction can 
be activated. See Chapter 5.11, 
“Processing-on-the-fly Functionality”, Page 79. 
• Option “3D” 
– Controlling a 3-Axis Scan System 
Allows that an RTC4 PCIe Board can 
synchronously control a third axis (z axis, for 
example, a varioSCAN as a dynamic focus unit) 
along with the scan system’s x axis and y axis 
(usually 2 galvanometer scanners) by its two 
scan head connectors. Otherwise, the associated 
3D commands are partially or fully unusable. See 
Chapter 5.10, “3D Functionality”, Page 75. 
• Option “Optical Data Interface” 
The optical data interface enables transmission of 
data by a polymer optical fiber linking the 
RTC4 PCIe Board to an optical interface 
(optionally) integrated in the scan system. 
See Figure 2 and Chapter 6.6, “Optical Data 
Interface (Optional)”, Page 89.
• Option “DC/DC Converter” 
These boards are equipped with an extra DC/DC 
converter (optoelectronic coupler) ex works. 
Then, the laser control signals LASERON, LASER1 
and LASER2 at the Laser Connector, Page 84 
(RTC4 Ethernet Board: LASER Socket Connector) 
are galvanically decoupled from the PC ground 
(RTC4 Ethernet Board: board potential and as a 
consequence from the power supply) 
Notes 
• To query which options are actually enabled on a 
board, get_rtc_version is used. 
• If you need one or more options which are not 
activated out of the factory on your 
RTC4 PCIe Board: order an corresponding license 
file incl. instructions how to apply the upgrade 
from SCANLAB, specifying the serial number and 
the options you want. 
• For retrofitting your RTC4 PCIe Board with the 
extra DC/DC converter (optoelectronic coupler) 
you need to send it to SCANLAB. 
• See also Chapter 8, “Accessories for the 
RTC4 PCIe Board”, Page 94.

Installation of the RTC4 PCIe Board is done in the 
following steps: 
(1) Checking Jumper Settings, Page 95 
(2) Installing the RTC4 PCIe Board, Page 99 
(3) Installing the RTC4 Board Driver, Page 100 
(4) Carrying-out Functionality Test, Page 100

9.1
Checking Jumper Settings
The factory settings for the jumpers are listed in the 
separate document listing the scope of supply. 
• If you do not need to change the factory settings, 
proceed with Chapter 9.3, “Installing the RTC4 
Board Driver”, Page 100.
The following jumpers can be configured, see 
Figure 1: 
• Jumper for the laser signal levels (X10, X11)
(active-high or active-low)
• Jumpers for the 9-pin D-SUB laser connector 
(X6, X7, X3)
• Jumpers for the 8-bit digital output port on the 
LASER EXTENSION socket connector (X8, X9) 
(optional)
Notice!
• RTC3 boards and RTC4 boards cannot be 
operated in the same PC. 
Caution!
• The jumpers are soldered junctions. If you need 
to change the jumper settings, you have to use 
a soldering-iron. 
Be careful not to damage the electronics on the 
board!

9.1.1
TTL Laser Signal Level
Make sure that the laser signal levels are set 
according to the specifications of your laser control. 
Observe the documentation of your laser.
The RTC4 permanently generates the following laser 
signals, see also page 48 through page 53:
• LASERON signal 
• LASER1 signal 
• LASER2 signal 
Provided jumper X11 is open – All laser signals can be 
set to either active-low or active-high logic by 
jumper X10: 
Active-low means that a logical 1 (“Laser On“, for 
instance) is represented by a low level (0 V, TTL). 
Active-high means a logical 1 is represented by a 
high level (+5 V, TTL).
9.1.2
Signals at the Laser Connector
The signals at pins (2) and (4) of the 9-pin 
Laser Connector, Page 84, see Figure 24, are set by 
the jumpers X6, X7 and X3. 
The following table shows the possible the jumper 
settings, see also the following Section “Setting the 
Jumpers”, Page 97.

Setting the Jumpers
Jumper X6 and Jumper X3 
4 If you want to use the signal ANALOG OUT1 
(for example, for lamp current control), set 
jumper X6 to Position 2-3. Then specify the 
output voltage range with Jumper X3, see table. 
4 Alternatively, the output signal at pin (4) can be 
set permanently to +5 V (jumper X6 in position 
1-2). In this case, jumper X3 can be ignored.
Jumper X7 
4 If you need the TTL signal LASERON for laser 
control, set the jumper X7 to Position 1-2. 
4 If your laser requires only the signals LASER1 
and LASER2, you can use pin (2) for the signal 
ANALOG OUT2 instead. To do this, set the 
jumper X7 to Position 2-3.

• The ANALOG OUT2 signal is also available 
by the MARKING ON THE FLY socket connector.

9.1.3
Digital Output Port 
(Laser Extension Socket 
Connector)
The 8-bit digital output port is available by the 
LASER EXTENSION socket connector, see Figure 25, 
odd numbered pins (1) to (17).
The port can be loaded with an 8-bit value 
by the commands write_8bit_port and 
write_8bit_port_list.
The pin (15) and pin (17) of the output port are 
configured by jumper field X8 and jumper field X9, 
see Figure 32. The most significant bit (L7) of the 
8-bit output value can be assigned to pin (15) or to 
pin (17). Alternatively, each of the two pins can be set 
permanently to +5 V (high level) or to GND 
(low level). 
Notes 
• If the L7 bit is assigned to pin (15), the full 8-bit 
output value is available on the output port 
(odd numbered pins (1) to (15) of the 
LASER EXTENSION socket connector).
• Setting pin (15) permanently to HIGH results in an 
offset of 128 for the output value and restricts 
the output values to (128…255).
• Setting pin (15) to LOW restricts the output value 
to 0…127.
• The L7 bit can be used for other purposes by 
assigning it to pin (17).
32
X8 jumper field and X9 jumper field. 
Caution!
• Make sure that only one position is closed in the 
X8 jumper field. Other combinations are not 
allowed and cause damage to the board! 
• Make sure that only one position is closed in the 
X9 jumper field. Other combinations are not 
allowed and cause damage to the board!

9.2
Installing the 
RTC4 PCIe Board
The RTC4 installing procedure is as follows:
(1) Remove all data storage media from your PC 
drives. 
(2) Shut down the operating system and switch off 
the PC. Disconnect the PC from the mains.
(3) Disconnect all peripherals such as monitor, 
mouse, keyboard, printer etc. from your PC.
(4) Move the PC to an installation area that complies 
with the Electro Static Discharge (ESD) directives.
(5) Remove the housing of the PC. Locate a free PCIe 
slot and remove the slot cover. Make sure that 
there is at least 105 mm free space above the slot.
(6) Remove the RTC4 from the antistatic bag. Do not 
touch the contacts of the board.
(7) Install the RTC4 into the PCIe slot. Observe the 
instructions in the manual of your PC.
(8) Close the housing of the PC.
(9) Move the PC back to the work place and connect 
all peripherals.
(10)Connect the 25-pin D-SUB connector on the 
RTC4 to the scan head by a data cable. 
The pin-out of the D-SUB connector is shown in 
Figure 26. 
For the specifications and pin outs of the data 
cable, refer to Chapter 6.5, “Data Cable”, 
Page 88.
(11)Connect the 9-pin D-SUB connector on the RTC4 
to the laser by a suitable interface.
(12)Check all connections. 
(13)Turn the PC back on. 
Notice!
• RTC3 boards and RTC4 boards cannot be 
operated in the same PC. 
Caution!
• Always store the board in an electrostatically 
neutral environment in the supplied antistatic 
bag. 
• Carry-out the installation in an area that 
complies with the Electro Static Discharge (ESD) 
directives. Follow the instructions in your PC 
manual when installing the board. 
• Do not touch the electrical contacts of the 
board. 
• Protect the board from humidity, dust, corrosive 
vapors and mechanical stress.

9.3
Installing the RTC4 Board 
Driver
For installing the RTC4 driver for Windows 10, 8, 7 
proceed as follows:
• After the RTC4 board has been inserted, start the 
PC.
For Windows 7, Vista, XP:
(1) If the “Add Hardware Wizard“ does not open 
automatically, start it from the Control Panel.
(2) In the “Add Hardware Wizard“ specify the 
directory that contains the RTC4 drivers. During 
installation, the operating system automatically 
selects the appropriate driver file (32 or 64 bit).
For Windows 10, 8:
(1) If the “Add Hardware Wizard“ does not open 
automatically, start it from the Control Panel.
(2) Open the Device Manager to display the device 
tree. Look for the “PCI device” entry and update 
its driver by specifying the directory that contains 
the RTC4 driver.
Notes 
• In addition to the RTC4 driver, the RTC4 software 
(DLL, correction files and DSP Program File(s)) 
must be installed. 
Follow the instructions in Section “Software for 
the RTC4 PCIe Board”, Page 101.
9.4
Carrying-out Functionality 
Test
The HPGL Converter HPGL.exe is supplied for testing 
control of the scan head with RTC4 PCIe Boards (also: 
V2 RTC4 Ethernet Boards). 
HPGL.exe lets you load graphics files in Hewlett 
Packard HPGL format (vector graphic plotter files 
*.plt) for transfer to the RTC4 PCIe Board. 
(1) Copy HPGL.exe and the supplied *.plt into the 
same directory as the DLL, the 
DSP Program File(s) and correction file(s). 
(2) Start HPGL.exe.
(3) Choose Options > Correction, and then select a 
correction file. 
(4) Choose File > Load HPGL-File, and then select a 
*.plt. 
(5) To start output, choose Mark > Start Marking. 
Notice!
• RTC3 and RTC4 boards cannot be operated in 
the same PC. 
Caution!
• Always turn on the RTC4 and the power supply 
for the scan head first before turning on the 
laser. Otherwise, there is the danger of 
uncontrolled deflection of the laser beam.

10 Software for the RTC4 PCIe Board
10.1 Installing the Software
The DLL file for RTC4 32 bit applications is 
RTC4DLL.dll and whereas RTC4DLLx64.dll is for 64 bit 
applications. They support the RTC4 PCIe Board 
under the Microsoft operating systems Windows 10, 
8, 7. The DLL provides all necessary functions for 
operating the RTC4 PCIe Board. 
To install the software for the RTC4 PCIe Board, 
follow these steps:
(1) Copy the DLL (RTC4DLL.dll or RTC4DLLx64.dll(1)) 
included in the software package for the 
RTC4 PCIe Board to the directory in which the 
user program is started, or to the Windows 
directory.
(2) Copy the correction file(s) (*.CTB) and the RTC4 
DSP Program File(s) (*.HEX) to the hard drive of 
your PC (existing correction files can still be used; 
do not overwrite customized correction files!). 
SCANLAB recommends storing these files in the 
directory in which the user program is started.
(3) In order to use the commands and functions of 
the DLL in your user program, you need to 
initialize them. Chapter 10.2 describes the calling 
convention of the DLL and shows how to initialize 
the functions and procedures of the DLL in 
user programs written in Pascal, Visual Basic, 
Visual Basic .NET or C. 
(1) The RTC4DLLx64.dll requires the RTC4 driver version 
6.1.7600.16385 (March 18, 2016) or newer, see 
Readme.txt.
To identify the current driver version, open Windows 
Device Manager (for example, by Start > Run > 
devmgmt.msc > RETURN KEY). Open the folder 
’SCANLAB controllers’, right-click the desired RTC 
device (for example, 
RTC4: Real Time Scanner Controller) and choose 
Properties from the context menu. On the tab Driver 
the driver details are shown (and for users with 
granted local administration rights the enabled button 
Update driver for installing a newer driver).
Caution!
• Carefully check your user program before 
running it. Programming errors can cause a 
break down of the system. In this case, neither 
the laser nor the scan head can be controlled.

[p.102]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
10 Software for the RTC4 PCIe Board 
102
innovators for industry
Caution!
• For Windows PCs with 
Driver version 6.1.7600.16385 
Driver date March 18, 2016 (or newer) 
installed(a), the following must be observed: 
If the RTC4 PCIe Board is not in use by an 
RTC4 application, there is no problem to turn on 
or of its power supply.
Once the RTC4 PCIe Board is in use by an 
RTC4 application, its power supply must not be 
turned off under any circumstances. Otherwise, 
all non permanent settings of the 
RTC4 PCIe Board are lost. 
The power supply for the RTC4 PCIe Board is 
switched off in the Windows modes “Sleep” and 
“Hibernate”.
Therefore, the RTC4 driver is engineered to 
prevent these modes – if they are automatically 
initiated(!) by Windows - whenever the 
RTC4 PCIe Board is in use by an 
RTC4 application. 
In contrast, the RTC4 driver cannot prevent 
these modes, if they are manually started (that 
is, if a user chooses Sleep or Hibernate from the 
Windows Start menu). This action has priority 
and switches Windows into the chosen mode in 
any case. As a consequence, the power supply 
of the RTC4 PCIe Board is disconnected.
Therefore, Sleep and Hibernate commands in 
the Windows Start menu should be disabled 
(see Figure 33) for all users of the machine (at 
least when the RTC4 PCIe Board is in use by an 
RTC4 application).
As there are several ways to achieve this 
(for example, the Windows built-in command-
line utility Powercfg.exe or “Power Options” in 
Windows Control Panel,) you need to consult 
your system administrator.
(a) To identify the driver, see footnote on page 19.
33
Windows Start menu (example Windows 7): The menu 
commands Sleep and Hibernate shall be disabled for all 
users while an RTC4 application executes with 
driver version 6.1.7600.16385, 
driver date March 18, 2016 (or newer). 
Caution!
• For Windows PCs with 
Driver version 6.1.7600.16385 
Driver date March 18, 2016 (or newer) 
not installed(a)(b), the following must be 
observed.
The RTC4 PCIe Board does not support power-
saving modes, which switch off the power 
supplies of the PCIe bus. According standby or 
sleep modes of the operating system must be 
deactivated. Especially deactivate the Windows 
sleep mode option (some Windows operating 
systems have activated this option by default).
(a) For example, driver V.2.2.0.
(b) To identify the driver, see footnote on page 19.

10.2 DLL Calling Convention
The DLL calling convention is stdcall.
To facilitate importing the commands of the DLL into 
a Pascal, Visual Basic, Visual Basic .NET or C 
application, the software package for the 
RTC4 PCIe Board contains corresponding utility files.
The following sections describe how to use these files 
in different software development systems.
10.2.1 Pascal
Use the file RTC4Import.pas as a unit and call the RTC4 
commands you need, like
goto_xy(1000, 2500);
for performing a jump to location 1000, 2500.
10.2.2 Visual Basic
Include file RTC4Import.bas into your project and call 
the RTC4 commands you need, like
call goto_xy(1000, 2500)
for performing a jump to location 1000, 2500.
10.2.3 Visual Basic .NET
Include file RTC4Import.vb into your project and call 
the RTC4 commands you need, like
call goto_xy(1000, 2500)
for performing a jump to location 1000, 2500.
10.2.4 C
In C, you can choose either implicit linking – also 
known as static load or load-time dynamic linking – 
or explicit linking – also known as dynamic load or 
run-time dynamic linking.
Implicit Linking
To accomplishing implicit linking, include the header 
file RTC4impl.h and link with the (Visual C++) import 
library RTC4DLL.lib for building the executable.
Call the RTC4 commands you need like
goto_xy(1000, 2500);
for causing a jump to location 1000, 2500.
Explicit Linking
To accomplishing explicit linking, include the header 
file RTC4expl.h. Before calling any RTC4 function, 
initialize the DLL by calling the function RTC4open 
(which is defined in the file RTC4expl.cpp). 
When you are finished using the RTC4, close the DLL 
by calling the function RTC4close (also defined in the 
file RTC4expl.cpp).
For building the executable, link with the file 
RTC4EXPL.OBJ, which you can generate from the 
source code RTC4expl.cpp.
Call the RTC4 commands you need, like
goto_xy(1000, 2500);
for causing a jump to location 1000, 2500.
Notice!
• Some of the commands included in the utility 
files can only be used with the appropriate 
optional hardware configuration of the RTC4, 
see Chapter 7, “Options”, Page 93.
Make sure to use only the commands supported 
by your version of the RTC4. Refer to the 
descriptions of the individual commands in 
Chapter 11, “RTC4 Commands”, Page 113.
get_rtc_version provides information about 
the options currently installed on your RTC4 
board.

[p.104]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
10 Software for the RTC4 PCIe Board 
104
innovators for industry
Pros and Cons of Implicit and Explicit Linking 
10.2.5 Automatic Assignment of 
Access Rights
On opening the DLL by a user program (for example, 
by calling the function RTC4open for explicit linking in 
C),
• all existing RTC4 PCIe Boards are searched (up to 
16 RTC4 PCIe Boards can be used simultaneously 
in one PC),
• the user program is automatically assigned access 
rights to all found boards (as long as access rights 
are not already assigned to another 
user program),
• DLL-internal numbers are assigned for all found 
RTC4 PCIe Boards (important for multi-board 
commands) and
• one board is set as the active board (“working 
board“), which is the target for non-multi-board 
commands.
The RTC4 PCIe Boards cannot be simultaneously used 
by multiple user programs. 
To prohibit simultaneous access of several 
user programs to one RTC4 PCIe Board, only the 
firstly-opened DLL gets the access rights to all 
RTC4 PCIe Boards. 
If a user program opens an DLL and the access rights 
to the RTC4 PCIe Board have already been assigned 
to another user program, then load_program_file 
and load_correction_file return error code 8 and 
rtc4_count_cards returns 0. 
Subsequent changes to access rights are not possible. 
Changing the “working board” is done by select_rtc.
Multi-threading is supported. However, note that 
multiple threads of one user program can use the 
same board, but can not send commands to it at the 
same time (the DLL automatically serializes the 
command calls).
Usage of several RTC4 PCIe Boards by a single 
user program is described in Chapter 5.4, “Using 
Multiple RTC4 PCIe Boards in One PC”, Page 63. 
Implicit Linking 
Explicit Linking 
Necessary 
Files 
RTC4impl.h 
RTC4DLL.lib 
RTC4expl.h 
RTC4expl.cpp 
Pros 
Easiest linking 
method. 
No need to link the 
user program with 
an import library. 
Cons 
Need to link the 
user program with 
a compiler-specific 
import library. 
Need to initialize 
(RTC4open) and 
close (RTC4close) 
the DLL.

innovators for industry
10.3 Initializing the 
RTC4 PCIe Board
At the beginning of each RTC4 user program, you 
need to perform the following steps:
(1) Load the DSP Program File with the 
command load_program_file. The RTC4 signal 
processor starts automatically after this 
command.
(2) Load the correction file(s) to the RTC4 by 
load_correction_file, Page 153. 
See Chapter 5.3, “Using Two Different Correction 
Files”, Page 62, for information about using two 
different correction files.
(3) Set the laser mode by set_laser_mode.
(4) If necessary, assign the correction file(s) to the 
scan head control port(s). 
The default assignment (after each reset of the 
RTC4) is:
– The first scan head control port uses 
correction table #1.
– The second scan head control port is off.
Use the command select_cor_table to change to 
a different assignment.
(5) Define the scanner delay mode by 
set_delay_mode 
(“Variable Polygon Delay” or constant 
Polygon Delay).
(6) Load a table for the “Variable Polygon Delay” by 
load_varpolydelay, if necessary.
(7) Set the FirstPulseKiller length (YAG modes only) 
by set_firstpulse_killer.
(8) Set the stand-by pulses (usually CO2 Mode only) 
by set_standby.
(9) Load the list(s).
(10)If necessary, enable the external start input by 
set_control_mode.
The remaining settings such as laser timing, 
Laser Delays, Scanner Delays, jump speed and 
mark speed are set by list commands.

//

by using a CO2 laser

SCANLAB GmbH

//
Besides demonstrating of how to initialize the RTC4 and how to
//

//
link to the RTC4DLL.dll. For accomplishing implicit linking -
//
also known as static load or load-time dynamic linking of a DLL,
//
the header file RTC4impl.h is included and for building the
//
executable, you must link with the (Visual C++) import library
//
RTC4DLL.lib.
//
In case the operating system does not find the RTC4DLL.dll on
//
program startup, it will respond with a corresponding error
//
message and it will terminate the program.
//
//
Necessary Sources
//
RTC4impl.h, RTC4DLL.lib, MESSAGE.CPP
//
NOTE: RTC4DLL.lib is a Visual C++ library.
//
//

//
//
Compiler

- tested with Visual C++ 5.0 and Visual C++ 6.0
// system header files
#include <stdio.h>
#include <conio.h>
// RTC4 header file
#include "RTC4impl.h"
struct polygon{
short xval, yval;
};
#define R (20000)
polygon square[] = {
-R, -R,
-R,
R,
 R,
R,
 R, -R,
-R, -R
};
polygon triangle[] = {
-R,
0,
 R,
0,
 0,
R,
-R,
0
};
#define POLYGONSIZE(figure) (sizeof(figure)/sizeof(polygon))
void ErrorMessage(short ErrorCode);
void draw(polygon *figure, unsigned size);

void main(void *, void *)
{
short
ErrorCode;
printf("Polygon Marking with a CO2 laser\n\n");
// Initialize
ErrorCode = load_program_file("RTC4D2.HEX");
if(ErrorCode) {
printf("DSP Program File loading error: ");
ErrorMessage(ErrorCode);
return;
}
set_laser_mode(0);
// CO2 mode selected
set_standby(100*8,
// half of the standby period in 1/8 microseconds
8);
 // pulse width in 1/8 microseconds
ErrorCode = load_correction_file("Cor_1to1.ctb",
1,
// table; #1 is used by default
1.0, 1.0,
// scaling factor
0.0,
// rotation in degrees, counterclockwise
0.0, 0.0);
// offset in bits
if(ErrorCode) {
printf("Correction file loading error: ");
ErrorMessage(ErrorCode);
return;
}
// Timing, delay and speed preset
set_start_list(1);
set_laser_timing(100,
// half of the laser signal period
50,50,
// pulse widths of signals LASER1 and LASER2
0);
 // clock frequency; 0 corresponds to 1 microsecond.
// Otherwise, the clock frequency is 1/8 microseconds.
set_scanner_delays(25,
// Jump Delay in 10 microseconds
10,
 // Mark Delay in 10 microseconds
5);
 // Polygon Delay in 10 microseconds
set_laser_delays(100,
// laser on delay in microseconds
100);
// laser off delay in microseconds
set_jump_speed(1000.0); // jump speed in bits per milliseconds
set_mark_speed(250.0);
// mark speed in bits per milliseconds
set_end_of_list();
execute_list(1);
// Draw
draw(square, POLYGONSIZE(square));
draw(triangle, POLYGONSIZE(triangle));
// Finish
printf("Finished - press any key to terminate ");
while(!kbhit()) ;
(void)getch();
printf("\n");
return;
}
//
draw
//
//
Description:
//
//
Function "draw" transfers the specified figure to the RTC4 and invokes
//
the RTC4 to mark that figure, when the transfer is finished. "draw" waits
//
as long as the marking of a previously transferred figure is finished before
//
it starts to transfer the specified figure.
//

[p.109]
RTC4 Manual 
Doc. Rev. 1.9.1 en-US 
10 Software for the RTC4 PCIe Board 
109
innovators for industry
//
//
Parameter
Meaning
//
//
figure
Pointer to a polygon array
//
The first element of that array specifies the first location
//
from where the figure will be marked until the last location,
//
which is specified by the last array element.
//
//
size
Amount of polygons the polygon array contains
//
In case "size" equals 0, the function immediately returns
//
without drawing a line.
//
//
Comment
//
This function demonstrates the usage of a single list. Using list 1 only
//
means that you can utilize the space of both lists, which equals 8000 entries
//
totally.
//
//
NOTE
//
Make sure that "size" is smaller than 8000.
void draw(polygon *figure, unsigned size) {
unsigned short
busy, position;
unsigned
i;
if(size) {
do {
get_status(&busy, &position);
} while(busy);
// Only, use list 1, which can hold up to 8000 entries
set_start_list(1);
jump_abs(figure->xval, figure->yval);
for(i = 0, figure++; i < size - 1; i++, figure++)
mark_abs(figure->xval, figure->yval);
set_end_of_list();
execute_list(1);
}
}
